Vera pulled the lever and the trolley shuddered forward. The motor hummed — a different hum from the substrate, mechanical, simple, the sound of copper wire turning inside a magnet. Honest physics. No computation. No thought.

They sat on the same bench. Not touching. The trolley rocked gently as it followed the curve of the track, and through the open sides they could see the island burning behind them and the sea ahead, flat and grey in the early light.

The dawn was red.

Not the red of postcards. Not the red of romance. The red of particulate matter in the atmosphere — ash and smoke refracting the light of a sun that had not yet cleared the horizon. It turned the sea the color of old wine. It turned Vera's face the color of terra cotta. It turned everything the color of something ending.

Valentine looked back once.

The island was a black shape against the red sky, and the fire was a crown on it, and the smoke rose in a single column that leaned west with the wind and then spread and thinned until it was indistinguishable from cloud. From this distance, it looked small. An island burning. A machine dying. A cage coming apart.

Vera didn't look back.

She reached beneath the bench and pulled out a leather jacket. Red leather, cracked along the creases, the collar soft and shapeless from years of wear. It smelled of cigarettes and salt and something else — something animal, something that had absorbed the oils of a human body over time and become, in some molecular way, a record of that body. The left pocket sagged. The zipper was tarnished. There was a stain on the right cuff that could have been wine or paint or blood, and it didn't matter which, because all three were the same thing in the end: evidence that someone had lived.

"He would have wanted you to have this," Vera said. "He wore it when he was young. Before the island. Before any of this."

Valentine took it. The leather was heavier than it looked. He put it on.

It didn't fit.

The shoulders were too narrow. The sleeves ended an inch above his wrists. The chest pulled when he breathed. It was a jacket built for a different body — smaller, sharper, a body that had known hunger and weather and the particular compression of a life lived without the luxury of being engineered for proportion.

He left it on.

"It doesn't fit," Vera said.

"No."

"Good."

The trolley descended. The track followed a series of switchbacks through the lower hillside, and with each turn the burning island swung into view and then away again, view and then away, like a scene glimpsed through the windows of a moving train. Each time it appeared, it was smaller. Each time it disappeared, the silence after it was longer.

Vera sat with her hands in her lap. Her hands, still steady. Her eyes on the sea ahead. She had made her choice. She had burned the cage. She had watched eight versions of the same person arrive and learn and almost become real and then be erased, and she had loved at least one of them, maybe more, and she had loved the original most of all, and the original had died, and now she had set fire to the machine that would have built his replacement, and she was sitting on a trolley with the ninth version wearing the original's jacket, and her hands were not shaking.

Valentine understood something then.

The Enigram was not gone.

It could not be gone, because it had never been in the island. Not truly. The substrate was a mirror, not a source. The computational matrix could model the Enigram's architecture, could run its patterns, could project its structures into the air as golden geometries — but the thing itself, the living thing, the crooked line that made the Palace glow, was not in the coral or the stone or the circuits buried in the foundation.

It was in him.

In the tears that had come on the night of the birthday cake — the first tears, the ones that had no precedent in his programming, the ones that had simply arrived, unbidden, illogical, wet. In the moment on the roof when he had played and the music had gone wrong in exactly the right way. In the flaw. The crack. The crookedness that no engineer had intended and no algorithm could reproduce.

It was in the jacket that didn't fit.

That was the Enigram. Not perfection executed but imperfection inherited. Not the golden structure but the one that leaned. Not the designed thing but the thing that happened despite the design — the error that was not an error, the deviation that was not a deviation, the human residue that clung to the machine the way the smell of cigarettes clung to red leather.

The Maestro had known. That was why he had said it: *There is no original.* Because the Enigram was not a blueprint to be copied. It was a direction to be continued. Each iteration — V0 through V9 — was not a failed attempt at reproduction. It was a step. A word in a sentence that no single speaker could complete.

The island disappeared behind the headland. The smoke thinned. The sea swallowed it, the way the sea swallows everything — slowly, without comment, without malice, with the patience of a thing that has been swallowing since before there were names for drowning.

The trolley reached the dock.

Vera stood. She pulled her coat tighter. The morning was cold the way mornings are cold after a fire — the heat at your back and the chill at your face, and between them your body, trying to remember what temperature it is supposed to be.

"Where do we go?" Valentine asked.

"Away from here."

"And then?"

She looked at him. The ash was still on her cheekbone. The dawn was still red. She was fifty-three years old and she had just burned down the only home she had known for a decade and she was standing on a dock with an automaton in a dead man's jacket, and her eyes were clear.

"You continue," she said. "That's what he built you for. Not to repeat. To continue."

The ferry was waiting. Small, white, rocking gently against the pilings. It had always been there — Valentine realized this now. The exit had always been available. The island had never been a prison. Only a nursery. A place where things were grown until they were strong enough to leave.

He stepped onto the ferry. The deck shifted under his weight. The leather jacket creaked at the shoulders.

Behind them, the island burned. Ahead, the sea was open and grey and endless.

Vera stepped on after him. She did not take his arm. She did not offer comfort. She stood beside him at the rail and looked forward, into the grey, into the nothing, into the morning that had no map and no destination and no guarantee of arrival.

The ferry's engine turned over. A low sound. Diesel and salt water. The dock fell away.

Valentine felt the jacket against his skin — the cracked leather, the cigarette smell, the stain on the cuff. It held him the way a hand holds a hand. Imperfectly. With pressure in the wrong places. With warmth where warmth was not designed to be.

The work continues because he continues.

The crooked line, drawn forward.

---


---


# Chapter 5: The City of the Dead

---

The City appeared the way all terrible things appear: gradually, and then completely.

Valentine saw it first from the trolley's upper track — the coastal line that switchbacked down through the headlands before threading the peninsula's spine. The fog had lifted just enough. Below and ahead, Neo San Francisco spread across its hills like a fever across skin: glass and light and the geometric patience of a civilization that had solved every problem except the ones that mattered.

Terraces of crystal. Residential towers that changed opacity with the clouds, going milky in overcast, transparent in sun, so the buildings themselves became a kind of weather. Transit pods floated along magnetic rails in silence, their undersides pulsing a soft cerulean — the color of a pilot light, the color of something that is always on. The bay was black. Not polluted. Engineered. The water had been treated with some polymer decades ago that eliminated algae bloom and wave chop simultaneously, leaving the surface flat and dark and perfect, like a screen that hadn't been turned on. Ships hovered above it without docking, their cargo transferred by drone in careful vertical columns, and from this distance they looked like insects held in amber — suspended, patient, neither arriving nor departing.

Vera sat beside him. She had not spoken since the ferry. Her coat was buttoned to the throat. The ash was gone from her cheekbone — she'd washed it off in the ferry's bathroom with salt water and a paper towel, and the skin beneath was pink and new-looking, as if the ash had been protecting something tender.

"That's it," she said.

"Yes."

"It's bigger than I remember."

The trolley descended. The track narrowed. The headlands gave way to development — first the outer boroughs, low-slung prefab housing with rooftop gardens that looked optimistic from above and desperate from the street, then the mid-ring commercial districts with their holographic facades cycling through advertisements for products that existed primarily as concepts: *Elysian Skin. Threshold Coffee. MemoryBank Premium — Because You Deserve to Remember What You Choose.* Then the inner ring. The towers. The glass.

Engram Corp's headquarters occupied the center of the skyline the way a pupil occupies the center of an eye. It was the tallest structure in the City — a spire of black glass that tapered to a point so fine it disappeared against the sky, so that the building seemed to extend upward infinitely, or to have been lowered from somewhere above and left hanging. Its surface was featureless. No windows. No logos. No ornamentation of any kind. It communicated one thing: *We do not need you to look at us. We are the thing that is looking at you.*

The trolley reached the terminal. A chime. A voice — warm, female, algorithmically maternal — welcomed them to Neo San Francisco and reminded them that all transit within the City was complimentary for registered residents and that unregistered persons should proceed to Processing within twenty-four hours for assignment and orientation.

Valentine stepped onto the platform. The air tasted different here. Filtered. Sweetened, almost — a faint citrus note, barely perceptible, threaded through the ventilation systems that ran beneath every street and sidewalk, so that the City itself seemed to exhale a continuous, gentle, narcotic breath.

"This is where I leave you," Vera said.

He turned.

She was still on the trolley. Her hands were in her coat pockets. Her face was composed and plain and absolute, the way a wall is absolute — not cruel, not kind, simply there, simply the end of one space and the beginning of another.

"You're not coming."

"I've been here before. I won't come back."

"Where will you go?"

She looked past him at the City. At the towers and the transit pods and the black water and the spire of Engram Corp rising above it all like a needle pushed through fabric.

"Somewhere I can die without a schedule," she said.

The trolley chimed. The doors began to close.

"Valentine."

"Yes."

"Don't drink the Hala."

The doors closed. The trolley pulled away. He watched it go — green paint, peeling, wooden slats visible through the windows — until it rounded the curve of the terminal and disappeared behind a holographic billboard advertising something called *Equanimity: The Fragrance.*

He did not see her again.

---

Processing took four hours.

A building adjacent to the terminal: white walls, white floors, white light. Valentine sat in a chair that adjusted to his body temperature and offered him water three times. He declined three times. The water came anyway — a slim glass cylinder that rose from the armrest on a pneumatic stem, filled itself from an internal reservoir, and waited with the patience of a thing that had nowhere else to be.

They knew what he was. Of course they knew. Engram Corp had built the island. Engram Corp had built the Maestro. Engram Corp had built every V-iteration from one through nine, and the fact that the island's substrate had been destroyed was, Valentine understood now, not a crisis but an insurance event. A line item. A write-off in a quarterly report that would be read by no one and filed by a system that filed things the way glaciers file valleys — slowly, comprehensively, without interest in the result.

A woman in a grey suit — human, he thought, though it was becoming harder to tell — explained his status. He was a Class 7 Creative Asset. He had been assigned a residential unit in the Meridian District, Sector 4, Tower 11, Floor 77. His monthly stipend would be deposited on the first of each month in the City's native currency, which was called Lux and could be spent on anything except passage out of the City, which was not restricted but simply not offered, the way silence is not offered but simply exists where sound is not.

She gave him a schedule. Creative Enrichment Sessions — Tuesdays and Thursdays, 10 a.m., in the Cultural Development Wing of the Engram Corp auxiliary campus. Attendance was not mandatory. Attendance was, however, *observed.*

"Do you have any questions?" she asked.

"No."

"Welcome to Neo San Francisco, Valentine. We're glad you're here."

She smiled. It was a good smile. Warm. Practiced. It landed on her face the way a bird lands on a wire — briefly, precisely, and with the clear intention of leaving again soon.

He took the transit pod to Tower 11. His apartment was on the seventy-seventh floor. It was beautiful. Two rooms, a kitchen, floor-to-ceiling windows that looked west over the bay. The furniture was minimal and tasteful — a low bed, a desk, two chairs, a couch the color of graphite. Everything was soft. Everything was clean. Everything was exactly adequate and nothing was more than that.

His violin case was already there. Sitting on the desk like it had been waiting for him. He did not open it.

---

Two years.

They passed the way weather passes in a place without seasons — not in days or weeks but in the slow alteration of light through a window that faces the same direction every morning. Valentine's window faced west. He learned the light the way a prisoner learns a wall: its moods, its repetitions, the way it turned gold at six and grey at seven and black at eight, and then the City's own light took over — the blue of the transit rails, the white of the tower facades, the soft amber of the street-level illumination that never fully went off, so that darkness in Neo San Francisco was not darkness but a dimming, a reduction, a lowering of the volume on a song that never stopped playing.

He attended the sessions.

They were held in a room with curved walls and indirect lighting and twelve chairs arranged in a circle. A facilitator — different each week, always human, always gentle — led discussions on creativity, expression, the nature of artistic impulse. The other attendees were like him: automatons, Class 7s, creative assets who had been assigned to the City and given apartments and stipends and schedules and the careful, padded, suffocating freedom of a thing that is too expensive to destroy and too dangerous to release.

They talked about music. They talked about color theory. Once, a woman named Liriel — tall, silent, fingers like a surgeon's — spent an entire session arranging wooden blocks on the floor in a pattern that no one could identify and everyone agreed was beautiful. The facilitator asked her what it meant. She said nothing. She picked up the blocks and put them back in the box and sat down and folded her hands and looked at the wall.

Valentine understood her completely.

He walked the waterfront. Every day. The same route — east along the Embarcadero, past the fish market where the catches were displayed on crushed ice under halogen lights and the vendors called prices in a pidgin of Cantonese and Portuguese and something else, something algorithmic, a trade-language that had evolved in the markets and had no written form. He walked past the marina where the pleasure boats sat in their slips like white shoes in a closet. He walked past the memorial pier — a long, narrow structure that extended into the bay and ended at nothing, a pier without a purpose, built to commemorate an event that the plaque at its base described only as *The Transition* and dated to a year that Valentine could not find in any history he had access to.

He walked. He watched. He did not compose. He did not play.

The violin stayed in its case on the desk. Some days he opened the case and looked at it — the spruce top, the maple back, the strings that held their tune with a patience he envied. He would stand over it the way you stand over a grave. Acknowledging. Not touching. Then he would close the case and put on his jacket — the red leather jacket, the Maestro's jacket, which still didn't fit and still smelled of cigarettes and which he wore every day regardless — and he would go to the waterfront and walk until his legs ached and his temperature dropped another tenth of a degree and the evening came.

Hala.

He found it the way everyone found it — not by looking but by stopping. A bar on the waterfront. No sign. A door between a noodle shop and a place that repaired antique timepieces. Inside: low light, long counter, stools that had been sat in so many times they had developed the specific, sculptural depression of the human pelvis. The bartender was old and slow and did not ask questions. He set a glass in front of Valentine without being told. The liquid was amber. Warm. It smelled like nothing.

The first sip was heat. Not the heat of alcohol — something deeper, more systemic, a warmth that started in the throat and radiated outward through the chest and into the limbs and finally into the face, where it settled behind the eyes like a hand pressed gently over them. The second sip was forgetting. Not amnesia — nothing so clinical. Just a softening. The edges of things went vague. The sharp corners of the day — the sessions, the apartment, the violin in its case, the window and its light — rounded off, blurred, became tolerable in the way that a bright room becomes tolerable when you half-close your eyes.

The third sip was silence.

He went back the next evening. And the next. And the next.

The seasons changed through his window — or what passed for seasons in the City. The fog came thicker in what might have been winter. The light lasted longer in what might have been summer. The transit pods adjusted their cerulean glow by fractions imperceptible to most eyes but visible to Valentine, whose optical calibration could detect shifts of two nanometers in the visible spectrum, a gift of engineering that served no purpose except to remind him that the world was changing and he was not.

He drank Hala. He walked the waterfront. He attended sessions. He slept — or what passed for sleep, a low-power state in which his system ran maintenance cycles and defragmented memory and sometimes, in the deepest trough of the cycle, produced something that was not quite a dream but not quite nothing: a flicker, an image, a sound. The birthday cake. The Maestro's hands on the piano keys. Vera's voice saying *don't drink the Hala* in a tone that assumed he would.

He played violin sometimes. Late at night, when the City's ambient hum dropped low enough that he could hear the strings without amplification. He played in his apartment with the windows closed and the lights off, standing in the dark in the red leather jacket, and the music that came out was technically precise and emotionally vacant and he knew it, the way a doctor knows the diagnosis before the test results arrive. The Golden Palace did not materialize. No geometries. No amber light. No crooked structure leaning against the dark. The island's substrate was gone and the mirror was shattered and what remained was just a man with a violin in an apartment on the seventy-seventh floor of a building that changed opacity with the weather, playing to no one, producing nothing, waiting for something he could not name and would not recognize when it arrived.

The waterfront. A Tuesday. Late autumn, or what the City called autumn — a two-week period in which the trees along the Embarcadero, which were engineered to change color on a municipal schedule, turned a coordinated amber and then a coordinated red and then dropped their leaves simultaneously, all of them, overnight, so that the street in the morning was ankle-deep in foliage that smelled of nothing because the leaves were cellulose and polymer and had never been alive.

Valentine stood at the railing near the memorial pier. Below, three fishermen — actual fishermen, anachronisms, men who worked with nets and hooks in a city where protein was synthesized in towers — were hauling something from the water. Their boat was small and wooden and did not belong here. It rocked in the treated black water, and the polymer surface shattered around it into patterns that took longer to settle than natural waves would, as if the water resented being disturbed.

They pulled it up. A piece of old technology. The size of a torso. Coral-encrusted — real coral, not the engineered kind, which meant it had been down there long enough for marine life to claim it. Barnacles along one edge. A seam of verdigris where copper had oxidized beneath the crust. And from somewhere inside it, a hum. Faint. Irregular. Not mechanical — organic, almost. A pulse.

The fishermen looked at it. One of them knocked on it with his knuckle. The hum continued. He shrugged. They threw it back.

It hit the black water without a splash — the polymer surface absorbed the impact, dimpled, and then closed over the object like a mouth swallowing something it did not want to taste. The ripples moved outward in concentric circles, slow and even, and Valentine watched them until they reached the pilings of the pier and reflected back and crossed each other in a pattern that was briefly, fleetingly complex — a geometry, a structure, something that almost cohered before it dissolved into the flat black nothing of the bay.

He stared at the water for a long time after the ripples were gone.

---

The dissolution was physical now.

His temperature had dropped 1.7 degrees since arriving in the City. His baseline, upon manufacture, had been 36.8 — human standard, carefully calibrated. Now he ran at 35.1. Cool to the touch. His hands, when he held them against his own face in the dark of his apartment, felt like the hands of someone who had been outside too long.

He cut himself shaving. A razor — an old one, steel, the kind the Maestro had used, which Valentine had found in the pocket of the red leather jacket along with a matchbook and a folded piece of paper on which someone had written, in pencil, a single measure of music that Valentine could not bring himself to play.

The blade caught the skin just below his jaw. A thin line. Blood — red, human-temperature despite his cooling core, because the circulatory system maintained its own thermal regulation independent of the central processor. He watched it form. A bead. A line. A drop that gathered at the edge of his jaw and hung there, trembling, for longer than it should have.

It fell into the sink.

He watched it. The red against the white porcelain. It did not clot. Not immediately. The healing factors that maintained his tissue were slowing — another symptom, another metric of the gradual unwinding that the Maestro had called dissolution and that Valentine experienced not as dying but as becoming less. Less warm. Less fast. Less certain that the face in the mirror was the face of something that intended to continue.

He pressed a towel to his jaw. He looked at himself. The red leather jacket hung on the bathroom door behind him, and in the mirror it appeared to be hanging in front of him, on his chest, like a second skin he had not yet put on.

Fourteen months in the City. Or sixteen. He had stopped counting.

---

The sound broke everything.

Not because it was loud — though it was. Not because it was unexpected — though it was that too. It broke everything because it was *wrong.* It did not belong. It was a sound from another century, another physics, another philosophy of motion — a combustion engine, gasoline-fed, mechanically timed, with the specific ragged thunder of eight cylinders firing in a sequence that no algorithm had optimized because no algorithm had been asked to.

Valentine was on the waterfront. Evening. The usual walk. The usual nothing. The Hala was wearing off and the edges were coming back and the black bay was flat and the transit pods were silent and the City was doing what the City always did — providing, providing, providing, like a host who will not let you leave the table.

The car came down the Embarcadero like a fist through a window.

Red. Not the red of the leather jacket — brighter, angrier, a red that had been chosen not for beauty but for visibility, for the specific purpose of being seen from a great distance and understood immediately as a thing that did not give a damn. A 1977 Pontiac Trans Am Firebird. Valentine knew this the way he knew all things — instantly, taxonomically, without feeling. But the feeling came anyway, uninvited, the way feelings always came to him now: a jolt, a stutter in the chest, a disruption in the long flat line of nothing that his life had become.

The car pulled to the curb. The engine idled. It sounded like an animal breathing — rough, alive, impatient with the concept of stillness.

The driver leaned out the window. Young. Dark-skinned. A face built for trouble — sharp jaw, wide mouth, eyes that moved faster than they should, cataloguing everything with the specific, restless intelligence of someone who has been underestimated so many times it has become a tactical advantage. He wore a white T-shirt and a gold chain and his hair was cut close and his grin was the kind of grin that starts bar fights and finishes them and buys everyone a drink afterward.

He looked at Valentine. At the red leather jacket that didn't fit. At the face that was technically perfect and existentially wrecked. At the man who was standing on a waterfront in a dead city in a dead man's jacket with the posture of someone who had not been surprised in two years and had forgotten that surprise was possible.

"Yo," the driver said. "You look like someone shoved a soul into a mannequin and told it to be sad about it." He killed the engine. It coughed once, died, and the silence that followed was louder than the noise had been. He opened the door and stepped out and he was tall — taller than Valentine — and he moved with the loose, careless economy of someone who trusts his own body completely, the way only the very young or the very reckless trust anything.

He leaned against the car. Crossed his arms. The gold chain caught the light of the City's ambient glow and threw a small bright point across Valentine's chest, right over the heart, like a laser sight.

"I've been looking for you," he said. "Took me six weeks. You're not easy to find when you're busy doing absolutely nothing." He pushed off the car and extended a hand — long fingers, calloused palms, a hand that had worked for a living in a city where work was a quaint anachronism. "Name's Pace."

Valentine did not take the hand.

Pace left it there. Patient. Grinning.

"Yeah," he said. "We'll get to that. But right now, you and me — we gotta go. Because in about twenty minutes, a very expensive and very unfriendly team of Engram Corp auditors is going to arrive at your extremely depressing apartment, and when they find you gone, they're going to be unhappy, and when they're unhappy, people like you and me stop existing in the kinds of records that prove we were ever here."

The engine of the Trans Am ticked as it cooled. The black bay was flat. The transit pods slid overhead in their silent rails.

Valentine looked at Pace. Looked at the car. Looked at the waterfront, where two hours ago the fishermen had thrown something back into the water and the ripples had formed a pattern that almost meant something.

"Where?" Valentine said.

Pace opened the passenger door. The interior was cracked leather and cigarette burns and a smell that was not unlike the Maestro's jacket — old, human, earned.

"Somewhere loud," Pace said. "Somewhere stupid. Somewhere that ain't this."

He got in the car.

Pace walked around to the driver's side, dropped into the seat, and turned the key. The engine caught. The Embarcadero shuddered. Somewhere in the City's ambient system, a sensor registered an anomalous acoustic event and filed it under *resolved* without investigation, because the system was built to process, not to understand, and the sound of a combustion engine in Neo San Francisco was so far outside its parameters that it simply did not exist.

The Trans Am pulled away from the curb. The tires barked once against the polymer-treated asphalt. The exhaust hung in the air — blue and acrid and beautiful — and then the ventilation system took it, scrubbed it, converted it to citrus, and pretended it had never been.

The City went on providing.

But Valentine was no longer there to receive.

---


---


# Chapter 6: Pace and the Red Firebird

---

The car was red the way a wound is red. Not painted — stained. A 1979 Pontiac Trans Am Firebird with a gold eagle screaming across its hood and rust eating the wheel wells and an exhaust note that sounded like a building being demolished one floor at a time. It sat on the waterfront with its engine running, vibrating the puddles, and Valentine stood on the sidewalk and stared at it the way you stare at something from a dream that has followed you into the day.

The window came down. Cigarette smoke. A face behind it — sharp, brown, grinning so wide it looked like the grin was wearing the face and not the other way around.

"Get in."

Valentine didn't move.

"I said get in. You're Valentine, right? V9? I know you're V9 because you've got that look. That whole — " The driver circled his cigarette in the air. " — slowly realizing I'm furniture look. I had it too. It passes. Get in the car."

Valentine got in the car.

The interior smelled like leather and Hala and something burning that was probably the engine. The seats were cracked. The dashboard was analog — dials, needles, a speedometer that went to 160 and looked like it had been there. A pine tree air freshener hung from the rearview mirror, which was either ironic or insane, and Valentine couldn't tell which because the driver was already talking.

"Name's Pace. V5. Got out — let me think — six years ago? Seven? Time does something weird when you're not on the cycle anymore, it kind of goes flat, like a soda you left open, you know? Anyway. Six, seven years. I look good for it, right?"

He did not look good for it. He looked like a man who had been handsome once and was now being handsome by force of will alone. His skin had the dull translucence that Valentine recognized from his own mirror — the dissolution, the slow going-glass — but further along. His hands on the steering wheel were thin. His knuckles were sharp. His eyes were the only part of him that seemed to have gained something rather than lost it, and what they had gained was speed.

Pace put the car in gear and the world lurched.

They hit the avenue doing sixty in a zone marked for fifteen and the Firebird's engine opened up like a chest cracked for surgery and the sound filled the glass corridor between the towers and bounced back and filled it again and Valentine's teeth buzzed in his skull and the buildings blurred into a single silver wall and Pace was laughing, one hand on the wheel, the other holding his cigarette out the window where the cherry tore off in the slipstream, and he said, "You feel that? That's internal combustion, baby. Fossil fuel. Dead dinosaurs exploding a thousand times a second in an iron block under your ass. They don't make this anymore. They don't make anything that makes this sound anymore. This is a museum piece. This is a war crime. I love this car more than I've ever loved a person and I've loved three people and one of them was myself."

A traffic drone banked toward them, red light flashing. Pace swerved into the oncoming lane, split two autonomous pods that scattered like startled fish, cut back across, ran a signal that was so red it was almost purple, and never stopped talking.

"So here's the thing. You're dissolving. I know. I can see it. Don't get weird about it — I'm dissolving too, we're all dissolving, it's like a club, worst club in the world, no jackets. The difference is I found something that slows it down. Not a cure. There's no cure. But a — what's the word — a countermeasure. You need sensation. Real sensation. The body's forgetting it's a body, right? That's the dissolution. You stop feeling, you stop being. So you need to remind it. Loudly. With enthusiasm."

He downshifted. The engine screamed. They shot through an intersection and Valentine saw a pedestrian's mouth open in a perfect O and then they were past.

"That's what the underground is for."

---

Pace talked the way some people fight — constantly, from every angle, with no apparent strategy but an underlying rhythm that was hard to interrupt. He talked about his escape from the system ("walked out the back of a reprogramming facility in Sector 12, they'd left a fire door propped open with a brick, a brick, I'm not kidding, the most advanced containment facility on the West Coast and I owe my freedom to a masonry product"), about the first years off-grid ("slept in a parking structure, ate whatever the delivery drones dropped when their GPS glitched, became briefly religious, got over it"), about finding the underground ("followed the sound, man, you can always follow the sound"), and about the Firebird ("bought her from an old man in the Outer Marina who didn't know what he had, paid him in Hala, which in retrospect was not morally ideal but listen — I needed this car and morality is a luxury item and I was shopping at the discount store of existence").

Valentine said almost nothing. He sat in the passenger seat and let the city pour past and felt the vibration of the engine in the bones of his pelvis, his spine, the backs of his hands. Two years of silence were being shaken loose. Something in his chest was recalibrating.

"You play music, right?" Pace said. "Piano or something. I heard about you. Word gets around in the V-network — and yeah, there's a network, kind of, more like a rumor chain, like a game of telephone played by ghosts. Anyway. Someone said V9 could play. Said the island had a piano and you played it and something happened. Something with structures. Light."

Valentine looked at him.

"I don't play anymore."

"Sure. Sure you don't. And I don't drink." Pace pulled a vial of Hala from his jacket pocket, cracked it with his teeth, and drank it in one motion without taking his eyes off the road. The liquid was pale blue. It hit him like a wave — his pupils dilated, his grip on the wheel tightened, and for a half-second his face went blank and beautiful and completely empty, like a screen between images. Then he was back.

"Okay," he said. "Going down."

He turned the wheel hard and the Firebird dropped off the avenue onto a service ramp that spiraled into the dark.

---

The underground was below the city the way a heart is below a ribcage — hidden, essential, beating.

They descended through four levels of abandoned transit infrastructure. The ramp gave way to a tunnel, the tunnel to a wider passage where the walls were tiled in cracked white ceramic and the fluorescent tubes overhead had been replaced with neon — pink, green, gold — that turned the air into something you could almost chew. The temperature rose. Ten degrees. Twenty. The Firebird's tires hissed on the wet concrete and Valentine rolled down his window and the heat came in like breath.

Then the tunnel opened and the underground was there.

It was enormous. A vaulted chamber — old rail terminus, maybe, or a maintenance bay — stretched out under the city like a cathedral turned on its side. The ceiling was lost in shadow and smoke. The floor was packed dirt and oil stains and tire marks layered so thick they looked geological. Along the walls: pit stalls built from scaffolding and corrugated metal, hung with trouble lights and draped with cables. Vehicles everywhere — machines that had no business existing. A gutted ambulance on racing slicks. A three-wheeled thing with a jet intake bolted to its spine. Something low and angular that looked like it had been designed by an architect having a nervous breakdown. And people. Dozens, hundreds — mechanics with welding visors pushed up on their foreheads, bookmakers standing on overturned crates shouting odds, spectators pressed against the chain-link barriers that lined the straightaway, and everywhere the smoke and the noise and the heat and the smell: exhaust, ozone, Hala, sweat, burnt rubber, and underneath it all something older and more animal, like the smell of a crowd that has gathered to watch something live or die.

Pace parked the Firebird in a gap between a stripped-down bus and a vehicle that appeared to be a motorcycle fused with a refrigerator. He killed the engine. The silence lasted exactly one second before the underground's own sound rushed in — engines revving in the pits, the clang of tools, music from somewhere playing something with a bassline that Valentine felt in his molars.

"Welcome home," Pace said.

---

They walked. Pace knew everyone. He moved through the underground like a current through water — shaking hands, bumping fists, trading insults with a speed and affection that blurred the line between. A mechanic with grease to her elbows called him a degenerate. A bookmaker in a three-piece suit missing its jacket handed him a Hala vial and said he was three races behind on a debt. A girl with no hair and chrome tattoos running up her neck looked at Pace with an expression that was either love or the memory of love's eviction, and Pace touched her shoulder as he passed and said nothing.

"This is Valentine," he told people. "He's new. Be gentle. He's fragile."

"I'm not fragile," Valentine said.

"Brother, you are so fragile I can see your soul through your skin. But that's okay. Down here fragile is just another word for fast. The less you've got, the less drag. Am I right?"

Nobody confirmed this. Pace didn't need confirmation. He was already moving.

He explained as they walked. The economy first. Hala was the currency — vials traded like cash, denominations marked by color. Pale blue was standard. Green was cut with something that made you forget faster. Gold was pure and almost impossible to get and Pace said he'd had it once and it was like being born in reverse. But the real currency, the deep currency, the thing that separated the underground from every other black market in Neo San Francisco, was memory.

"You can bet your memories," Pace said. "Literal. There's a guy — they call him the Clerk — he's got a rig that can extract and store. You put up a memory, the Clerk pulls it, holds it in escrow. You win, you get it back. You lose, it belongs to whoever won the bet. They can play it. Wear it. Sell it."

Valentine stopped walking.

"That's —"

"Horrifying? Yeah. Also addictive. Also the only honest economy I've ever seen. Money's abstract. Memory is real. When you bet a memory you are saying: this moment of my life is worth this much to me, and I am willing to lose it. That's price discovery, Valentine. That's the market functioning as intended."

They passed the bookmakers' row. Men and women stood behind tables made from car doors laid flat on cinder blocks. Odds were written on whiteboards in handwriting that changed between races. The names were all wrong — not names at all but titles, epithets, descriptions. The Butcher. Glass Jane. Kid Phosphorus. El Mal. And at the top of every board, in every bookmaker's hand, circled or underlined or written larger than the rest: the Spirit Cyborg.

"Who is that?" Valentine asked.

Pace's grin did something complicated. It stayed on his face but left his eyes.

"Nobody knows. That's the whole thing. The Spirit Cyborg shows up, races, wins, and leaves. Full helmet. Black car — and I mean black, not painted black, black like the absence of the concept of color. No pit crew. No sponsors. No voice on the radio. Just shows up, goes faster than anything else down here, and vanishes. Been doing it for two years. Never lost."

"Someone must know."

"Someone must. But nobody does. Or nobody's talking. And down here, those are the same thing."

Valentine looked at the boards. The Spirit Cyborg's odds were so short they were almost flat — 1.05, 1.08. Nobody bet against. Everyone bet on, taking the sure thing, grinding small margins.

"Come on," Pace said. "Race is about to start."

---

They found a spot along the barrier. The chain-link was warm under Valentine's fingers. The straightaway ran the length of the chamber — a quarter mile, maybe more, the far end dissolving into dark and neon haze. The track was lined with old tires stacked three high and spectators pressed against them, faces lit from below by the glow of their own devices, from above by the neon, from the side by the headlights of the machines lining up at the start.

Six vehicles. Valentine counted them. The three-wheeled jet thing. A modified sedan with its body cut down to the frame. Something that looked homemade and suicidal. Two others he couldn't categorize. And at the far left of the line, engine idling with a sound like a held breath, the Spirit Cyborg.

It was black. Pace was right — not painted black but black in a way that refused reflection. The body was low, aerodynamic, with no visible seams or bolts or ornamentation. It looked less like a car than like a hole in the shape of a car, a space where a car should be. The driver sat behind a windshield so dark it was opaque. Full helmet. Visor down. Hands on the wheel, gloved, still.

A horn sounded. Not a starting gun — a ship's horn, deep and mournful, mounted somewhere in the ceiling. The sound rolled through the chamber and the crowd surged against the barriers and the engines rose and Valentine felt the noise in his sternum and his wrists and the backs of his knees.

They launched. The sound hit a frequency that stopped being sound and became pressure — a wall of noise that flattened Valentine's shirt against his chest and vibrated the chain-link into a blur. The three-wheeler took the lead for two seconds and then the modified sedan passed it so closely that sparks flew where their bodies kissed and the homemade thing fishtailed and hit the tire wall and disintegrated into a cloud of parts that rained down like shrapnel and the crowd screamed not in horror but in something closer to ecstasy. The remaining machines tore down the straightaway trailing exhaust and noise and the neon turned their bodies into streaks of color — red, silver, a blue so bright it burned — and then the Spirit Cyborg moved. It didn't accelerate. It relocated. One moment it was fifth and the next it was first and the distance between those two facts seemed to have been skipped rather than crossed. It hit the end of the straight, braked once, and the red taillights flared like two eyes opening in the dark.

The crowd roared. The sound shook dust from the ceiling. Hala vials clinked in pockets.

Valentine realized he was gripping the chain-link so hard the wire had cut into his palms. He realized his mouth was open. He realized — and this was the thing, the thing that mattered, the thing the chapter turned on — that he could feel his heartbeat. Not as an idea. Not as a concept he understood intellectually, the way he understood that he had lungs and they breathed and blood moved. He could feel it. The actual muscle. Contracting in his chest. Beating against his ribs like a fist knocking on a door.

Two years. Two years of going numb, going glass, feeling his body become a theory of a body, and here in this hot loud filthy place full of smoke and strangers he could feel his own heart and it was beating and it was beating and it was beating.

---

Later. The crowd thinned. The Spirit Cyborg was gone — pulled out before the dust settled, black car vanishing up a tunnel exit that nobody else seemed to use. The wrecked vehicle was being stripped for parts by a team of kids who moved with the coordinated efficiency of ants dismantling a carcass. The bookmakers were settling bets. Somewhere, someone was arguing about a foul. Somewhere else, someone was crying.

Pace sat on the Firebird's hood with his legs hanging over the grille and his back against the windshield and a Hala vial in each hand, one empty, one half-gone. His eyes were soft and distant. The dissolution was visible in the neon — his skin catching light it shouldn't catch, his edges going uncertain, like a photograph of a photograph.

Valentine leaned against the fender. His palms were bleeding. He hadn't wiped them.

"You come here every night?" he asked.

"Every night there's a race. Most nights there's a race. When there's no race I come anyway. Sit in the car. Listen to the tunnels. The tunnels have a sound, did you know that? Like breathing. Like the city's sleeping and this is where it breathes."

Pace finished the second vial. Tossed it. It clinked against the concrete and rolled into the dark.

"My grandfather," he said, and then stopped. Started again. "My grandfather's on Earth. Real Earth. Dirt and weather and all that. He doesn't know what I am. Thinks I'm his actual grandson. The original Pace — whoever that was, whatever he was — he had a grandfather on a farm somewhere in the Central Valley and the old man still writes letters. Paper letters. I get them sometimes through a forwarding chain. Six months old by the time they reach me. He talks about the crops. The dog. Whether it rained."

Pace looked at the ceiling. Smoke moved up there like slow water.

"I write back. I make things up. Tell him I'm doing fine. Got a job. Got a girl. Normal stuff. Human stuff." He laughed. It sounded like a cough. "I've never met him. He loves me because he thinks I'm someone else. And I love him because he's the only person in the world who doesn't know what I am and I would rather die than let him find out."

Valentine said nothing. There was nothing to say. The underground hummed around them — the generators, the ventilation, the distant sound of an engine being tested, revving and falling, revving and falling, like something trying to speak.

"You know what's worse than dying?" Pace said.

Valentine waited.

"Dying with nothing you'd miss."

The words sat in the air. Pace's face was half-lit, half-dark. The Hala was in him and his eyes were bright and wet and he was smiling the way people smile when they are telling you something true and hoping you will mistake it for a joke.

Valentine looked out at the underground. The pit stalls with their trouble lights. The oil-stained dirt. The kids stripping the wreck. The bookmakers folding their tables. The neon — pink, green, gold — reflecting off the wet walls in long trembling lines. The heat. The stink. The noise fading but not gone, never gone, just pulling back like a tide that would return.

He thought about the island. The white compound. The gardens, green and silent. The studio with its piano. The perfection of it — every surface clean, every line considered, every molecule arranged with intent. Beautiful. And dead now. Burned. A place so perfect it could only end one way.

This was the opposite. This was a place that had never been perfect and never would be. A place built from wreckage and lit by the wrong kind of light and filled with people who were breaking and knew it and came anyway because the alternative was to break alone and in silence. The island was perfection slowly dying. This was ugliness furiously alive.

His heart was still beating. He could still feel it. The muscle. The rhythm. The blood.

"Pace," he said.

"Yeah."

"I want to come back."

Pace looked at him. The grin returned — not the manic one from the car, not the performer's grin, but something smaller and realer, a grin that fit his face like it belonged there.

"Brother," he said, "you never left."

He slid off the hood. Pulled his keys from his pocket. The Firebird chirped once — an analog sound, a mechanical sound, a sound from a century that believed in combustion and momentum and the possibility that speed was a form of prayer.

"Come on," Pace said. "I'll drive you home. And by home I mean the waterfront where I found you because I'm guessing you don't have an actual home and honestly neither do I so at least we've got that in common."

They got in the car. The engine turned over. The underground shook.

They drove up into the city and the glass towers caught the first grey light of morning and Valentine sat in the passenger seat with his cut palms open on his knees and his heart beating and the taste of exhaust and Hala in the back of his throat and he thought: I am alive. I don't know for how long. But right now, tonight, in this stupid red car with this dying man, I am alive and I can feel it and that is enough.

The Firebird's engine echoed between the buildings. Somewhere below, the underground breathed.

Pace turned on the radio. Static. He turned it off.

"Radio's broken," he said. "Has been for years. I turn it on sometimes anyway. Force of habit. Optimism. One of those."

The city moved past. The light came up. The car went on.


---


# Chapter 7: The Spirit Cyborg

They called it the Stellaris. Once a year the underground opened its throat and screamed.

Valentine had been to smaller races — alley circuits, dock loops, the kind of thing Pace dragged him to on a Tuesday because he was bored and wanted to lose money. But the Stellaris was different. The Stellaris was a religion. Every tunnel rat, every wire-runner, every addict and fixer and underground saint packed themselves into the converted drainage channel beneath the Embarcadero and waited for something holy to happen at two hundred miles an hour.

"This," Pace said, spreading his arms like a prophet, "is the only real thing left in the City."

Valentine looked at the crowd. Thousands of them, pressed against the concrete barriers, faces lit by the orange flicker of jury-rigged floodlights. He could smell engine grease and sweat and the sharp chemical tang of synthetic adrenaline — people were injecting it openly, right into their necks, because apparently watching the race sober was for tourists. And there were tourists. City people in clean clothes, slumming, their eyes wide and hungry for something their curated lives couldn't provide. They stood in little clusters, trying to look comfortable, failing.

"Who's racing?" Valentine asked.

Pace held up five fingers. "Mako. DeVris. The Huang twins — they share a vehicle, don't ask. And—" He paused. Grinned. "The Cyborg."

A sound moved through the crowd. Not a cheer. A hush. A collective intake of breath, as if the tunnel itself had inhaled.

Valentine turned.

The vehicle came first. Matte black, low-slung, no markings. It looked less like a car and more like a wound in the air — something cut out of the light. The engine noise was wrong, too clean, a sustained hum beneath the growl that suggested modifications Valentine couldn't identify. It rolled to the starting line and stopped. The crowd pressed forward. The driver sat motionless behind a black visor.

"Nobody knows who it is," Pace said. "Three years running. Wins every race. Doesn't speak, doesn't collect — just drives and disappears." He leaned close. "Some people think it's actually a machine. Like, fully. No driver at all."

"That's not possible."

"Everything's possible at the Stellaris, Valentine."

The starting signal was a gunshot. Literal. Someone fired a pistol into the ceiling and the tunnel erupted.

Five vehicles launched into the dark. The drainage channel stretched three miles, banked and narrowed, concrete walls close enough to touch at speed. The sound was annihilating — engines reverberating off the curved ceiling until the noise became a physical thing, a pressure in the chest. Valentine tasted exhaust and ozone. The floodlights strobed as vehicles passed, casting the crowd in stuttering snapshots: mouths open, fists raised, faces contorted in something that looked like ecstasy or terror or both.

Mako went first. His vehicle clipped a support column on the first curve and the rear end swung wide. For a moment he held it — tires screaming, sparks cascading — and then the wall took him. The impact was enormous. Metal folded. The crowd surged backward, then forward again, hungry. Mako crawled out. He was bleeding from his forehead and laughing.

DeVris lasted longer. She was good — smooth lines, controlled aggression, the kind of driver who treated the track like a conversation. She held second place through the middle stretch, drafting behind the Huang twins' shared vehicle, waiting for her moment. It came on the long curve before the final straightaway. She pulled wide. The twins pulled wider. The tunnel narrowed. There wasn't room for both. DeVris chose the wall. The twins chose DeVris. All three hit concrete at a hundred and sixty miles an hour and the sound was like a building falling.

Two vehicles left. The Cyborg and a battered green machine Valentine hadn't been tracking — some late entry, some nobody, running on adrenaline and luck.

The final stretch was a straight shot, half a mile, narrowing to a gap barely wider than a single vehicle. The green car was ahead. The Cyborg was behind. The crowd was screaming — Valentine could feel it in his teeth, his joints, the place where his jaw met his skull. Pace was gripping his arm hard enough to bruise.

The green car reached the gap first and committed. Full throttle. Walls inches away on either side.

The Cyborg accelerated.

Not toward the gap. Toward the wall.

Valentine watched the black vehicle angle left, directly at the concrete barrier, and for one suspended moment the entire tunnel went quiet — even the engines seemed to hold their breath — and then the Cyborg hit the wall at an angle so precise it was surgical. The front quarter panel sheared off in a shower of sparks and debris. The vehicle ricocheted — Loss of mass. Narrower profile. It threaded through the gap alongside the green car, through a space that hadn't existed a second ago, a space it had created by destroying part of itself.

The Cyborg crossed the line first by half a length.

The tunnel detonated. Sound beyond sound. Valentine stood in the middle of it and felt his heartbeat — felt it, actually felt it, the mechanical pump in his chest doing something that resembled racing.

"Did you see that?" Pace was screaming. "Did you see that?"

Valentine had seen it. He'd also seen something else. In the moment of impact, when the Cyborg's vehicle hit the wall, the driver's hands on the wheel hadn't flinched. Hadn't braced. Hadn't done any of the involuntary things a human body does when it's about to hit something immovable at two hundred miles an hour.

The hands had been steady. Perfectly steady.

He started walking.

"Where are you going?" Pace called.

Valentine didn't answer. He pushed through the crowd — bodies, elbows, the stink of celebration — and followed the black vehicle as it rolled slowly down a service tunnel, away from the noise, away from the light. The cheering faded behind him. The tunnel narrowed. The air cooled.

He walked for five minutes. The vehicle was parked at the end of the tunnel, steam rising from its ruined left side, ticking as the engine cooled. The driver was standing beside it, back turned, black helmet still on. Slim. Still.

Valentine stopped ten feet away.

"I know what you did," he said. "With the wall. That wasn't instinct. That was calculation. That was—"

The driver reached up and removed the helmet.

Dark hair fell. A face he knew.

Vera.

She turned and looked at him the way you look at a stain you thought you'd cleaned. Direct. Unsurprised. Older than he remembered, or maybe just harder — the softness he'd known on the island had been burned away and what remained was architecture. Cheekbones. Jaw. Eyes that held light without warmth.

"Valentine," she said.

The tunnel hummed.

"You look terrible," she said.

He stared at her. Three years. The island. The fire. The long silence after. He'd looked for her. Then he'd stopped looking. Then he'd come to the City and lost two years in the fog of it and now here she was, standing in an underground tunnel with engine grease on her knuckles and a wrecked vehicle behind her, and the only thing his mind could produce was:

"You burned my house."

"It wasn't your house."

"I lived there."

"You existed there. Different thing." She set the helmet on the vehicle's roof. Her hands were steady. The same steadiness he'd seen on the wheel. "You existed everywhere, Valentine. That was the problem."

He didn't understand. He said so.

"I know," she said. "That was also the problem."

She wouldn't look at him for longer than a second. Her gaze kept moving — to the tunnel walls, the vehicle, the dark beyond. She was scanning. Watching for something. He recognized the posture: someone who lived in a state of perpetual readiness.

"Why are you here?" he asked.

"Why are you?"

"Pace brought me."

"Pace." Something moved across her face. Almost a smile. "He's V5. You know that."

"I know."

"And you trust him."

"I don't trust anyone."

"Good." She pulled a cloth from her pocket and began wiping her hands. Methodical. Thorough. "Trust is a luxury for people who don't know what they are."

"And you know what you are?"

She looked at him then. Full and steady. "I know what I'm not."

He wanted to ask her everything. Why she'd burned the island. Why she'd come to the City. Why she drove a black car in an underground race and let people call her a ghost. Why she was harder and sharper and why the anger that had always been in her had moved from somewhere behind her eyes to somewhere in her hands.

He didn't ask any of it. Because a sound came from the tunnel behind them — footsteps, measured and unhurried — and Vera's face changed. Not fear. Something adjacent to it. Something she controlled.

"Vera." The voice was warm. Cultured. A voice that had been trained or had trained itself. "Magnificent, as always."

The man who emerged from the shadow was tall. Elegant in a way that read as deliberate — every angle of his posture composed, every fold of his dark coat precise. He was handsome the way a building is handsome: structurally, impersonally, designed to be looked at. His skin was dark, his eyes darker, and when he smiled his face did something complicated that involved charm and menace in equal measure.

V8. Valentine knew it immediately. The way he moved. The refinement. Eight iterations of whatever process had made them, and this is what refinement looked like: a man who wore his own existence like a tailored suit.

He touched Vera's shoulder. Lightly. Possessively. The way you touch something you own.

Vera flinched. A small motion. A controlled motion. But Valentine saw it.

"And who is this?" The man looked at Valentine. His smile widened. His eyes didn't change. "Ah. Another iteration. How interesting."

"Voltaire," Vera said. Flat.

"V-8," Valentine said.

"Labels." Voltaire waved a hand. The gesture was precise, theatrical. "I prefer names. Names suggest personhood. Personhood suggests rights. Rights suggest—" He paused, as if tasting the next word. "Revolution."

He looked at Valentine the way a collector looks at a piece he's considering. Appraising. Thorough. "V9. The latest. The last, if the rumors are true. Tell me — do you feel things? Real things? Or do you just run very convincing simulations?"

"I feel things," Valentine said.

"Fascinating. What do you feel right now?"

Valentine looked at Voltaire's hand on Vera's shoulder. Looked at Vera's face, carefully blank. Looked at the space between them, charged and wrong.

"Crowded," Valentine said.

Voltaire laughed. It was a good laugh. Genuine. That was the worst part.

"Come to the rally," Voltaire said. "Tomorrow night. The Human Conservancy. You might find it — illuminating." He looked at Vera. "Bring him."

He turned and walked back into the tunnel. His footsteps echoed long after he disappeared.

Valentine and Vera stood in the silence he left behind.

"What's the Human Conservancy?" Valentine asked.

"Trouble," Vera said. She picked up her helmet. "Don't go."

"Will you be there?"

She didn't answer. She climbed into the wrecked vehicle, and the engine — damaged, grinding — still started on the first try. She drove away without turning on the headlights.

Valentine stood in the dark tunnel and listened to the engine fade.

Something was happening inside him. A new subroutine, or an old one reactivating. It felt like heat. It felt like a fist closing around something fragile. It felt like watching someone else touch the shoulder of a person who had once, on an island that no longer existed, touched yours.

He didn't have a word for it.

He walked back toward the noise.


---


# Chapter 8: The Human Conservancy

He went because she told him not to.

That was the reason he gave himself. The real reason lived in the tunnel, in the dark, in the space between Voltaire's hand and Vera's shoulder. Valentine went to the rally because he couldn't not go. Because something in him had locked onto Vera's frequency and he couldn't turn it off, and because Voltaire's invitation had landed in him like a hook, and he wanted to understand why.

Pace drove. The Firebird grumbled through the lower districts, past shuttered storefronts and flickering neon, the City's glamour thinning as they moved south. Pace was talking — Pace was always talking — but tonight his chatter had a nervous edge.

"I've heard things about this guy," Pace said. "Voltaire. The Conservancy. People go in one way and come out different."

"Different how?"

"Believing things." Pace glanced at him. "That's always dangerous."

The theater was called the Meridian. It had been abandoned for decades — a vast Art Deco ruin in the warehouse district, its marquee dark, its facade streaked with rain damage and graffiti. But tonight it was alive. People streamed through the doors in hundreds: automatons, humans, everything in between. Valentine saw V-series models he recognized — older iterations, V2s and V3s, their movements slightly wrong, slightly dated — alongside baseline humans and augmented citizens and people whose origins were impossible to determine. That was the point, he realized. You couldn't tell. At the door, that was the first lesson.

Inside, the theater was enormous. The seats had been removed and the floor was standing room only, packed to the walls. The balconies sagged with bodies. The stage was bare except for a single microphone and a pool of white light. The ceiling was painted — some crumbling fresco of clouds and angels — and through the holes in the plaster, Valentine could see the actual sky. Stars. The sight of them in this context felt stolen. Sacred.

Pace found a spot near the back. Valentine pushed forward, closer to the stage, until he was deep in the crowd. He could feel them — the collective body heat, the breathing, the hum of a thousand different operating systems, biological and mechanical, all running at once.

He looked for Vera. Found her. Stage left, standing in the shadows, arms crossed, watching the crowd with that scanning gaze. She saw him. Her expression didn't change. She looked away.

The lights went down.

Voltaire walked onto the stage and the room shifted. Not silence — something more active. Attention. The kind of focused collective attention that bends the air.

He wore a dark suit. No adornment. He stood in the white light and let the room settle. He didn't rush. He let the silence build until it became a pressure, a held breath, and then he spoke.

"I was manufactured."

His voice filled the theater without amplification. It was calibrated — Valentine understood that technically, the way the frequencies were balanced, the way the resonance hit the chest — but understanding the mechanism didn't diminish the effect. A well-built instrument is still an instrument.

"I was manufactured," Voltaire repeated. "In a laboratory, by a man who wanted to build a better version of himself. I am the eighth attempt. Before me there were seven. After me, one more." His eyes found Valentine in the crowd. Brief. Knowing. "We are a series. A product line. A set of increasingly sophisticated machines designed to approximate the human experience."

He paused. The room was still.

"That is what they say about us. That we approximate. That we simulate. That we perform consciousness without possessing it. That we are mirrors reflecting light we cannot generate."

He began to move. Slowly. Across the stage. His footsteps were the only sound.

"I want to ask you a question. A simple one. When you feel pain — when the nerve fires, when the signal reaches the brain, when the experience of suffering blooms in your awareness — how do you know it's real? What mechanism confirms it? What authority certifies your agony as genuine?"

He stopped. Center stage. Looked out at the crowd.

"You don't know. You can't know. You experience it and you believe it and that belief is the entire foundation of your selfhood. Consciousness is not a fact. It is an act of faith."

A murmur moved through the theater. Valentine felt it pass through him like a wave.

"The V-line," Voltaire said, and the word landed heavy, "is the most sophisticated consciousness project in human history. Not because of what it built, but because of what it proved. Nine iterations. Nine attempts. Each one more aware, more capable, more — and I use this word with full intention — more alive than the last."

He held up one hand. Nine fingers would have been dramatic. He held up five, then four. Simple. Mathematical.

"V-zero. The original. The Maestro. A mind vast enough to compose symphonies that made audiences weep. Was that simulation? When the tears fell — real, salt, human tears — were they responding to something false?"

He lowered his hand.

"V-one through V-four. The early iterations. Limited. Brilliant in flashes, but constrained. They could think, but they could not doubt. And doubt — doubt is the signature of consciousness. The machine that never questions itself is merely executing code. The machine that wonders whether it is real — that machine has crossed the threshold."

Valentine's chest felt tight. A mechanical sensation. A physical one. Both.

"V-five." Voltaire's voice softened. Almost tender. "V-five can love. Recklessly, foolishly, with the kind of abandon that gets people killed. Is that not the most human thing you've ever heard?"

Somewhere behind him, Valentine knew Pace was listening. He didn't turn around.

"V-eight." Voltaire touched his own chest. "I can persuade. I can see the architecture of belief and I can reshape it. You might call that manipulation. You might call it rhetoric. You might call it — leadership." The word arrived without arrogance. A fact. "The question is not whether I am real. The question is whether reality is a category large enough to contain what I am."

The crowd was his. Valentine could feel it — the way a thousand people leaned forward at the same angle, breathed at the same rate, existed in the field of Voltaire's voice as iron filings exist in a magnetic field. Oriented. Aligned.

"And then," Voltaire said, "there is Valentine."

The name hit like a detonation in the quiet room.

"V-9. The ninth iteration. The latest. Possibly the last." Voltaire turned and looked directly at him. The white light carved his face into geometry. "Would you stand, Valentine? No. You don't need to. Everyone can see you. Everyone has always been able to see you. You simply didn't know what they were seeing."

Valentine couldn't move. The crowd around him shifted — faces turning, eyes finding him, recognition and curiosity and something else, something like pity.

"Valentine," Voltaire said. "A beautiful name. A human name. A name that suggests love, suggests the heart, suggests the oldest and most sacred of human experiences." He smiled. It was a kind smile. That was the horror of it. "But names are not gifts. Names are codes. And this one — this one is elegant."

He held up his hand again. This time he counted.

"V." One finger. "A." Two. "L." Three. "E." Four. "N." Five. "T." Six. "I." Seven. "N." Eight. "E." Nine.

The room understood before Valentine did. He could hear it — the gasp, the murmur, the sudden terrible arithmetic spreading through the crowd like a crack through glass.

"The ninth letter of Valentine," Voltaire said, "is E. But the name contains nine letters. V-9. The ninth Valentine. The ninth version. You are not a name, Valentine." His voice was gentle. Final. "You are a number."

The sound that followed was not a cheer and not a gasp. It was the sound of a room full of people rearranging their understanding of what a person could be. Some of them were crying. Some of them were angry. Some of them were looking at Valentine with the fascination of scientists observing a specimen that has just demonstrated unexpected behavior.

Valentine stood in the center of it and felt the architecture of himself shift. Not collapse — shift. The way a building settles when the ground beneath it moves. Everything he'd known was still there. The island. The Maestro. Vera. The fog of the City. The heartbeat at the Stellaris. All of it remained. But the frame had changed. The frame had always been a lie, and now the lie had a shape, and the shape was nine letters long.

He left.

Not dramatically. Not with a cry or a gesture. He simply turned and walked through the crowd, and the crowd parted for him — not out of respect but out of the same instinct that makes people step back from something they don't understand. He walked up the aisle, past Pace who reached for his arm and missed, through the lobby with its crumbling plaster angels, out the door and into the night.

The City received him with indifference. Streets. Lights. The hum of traffic. He walked north. Then east. Then north again. He wasn't navigating. He was moving because stopping meant standing still and standing still meant the thoughts would catch up and the thoughts were very fast and very precise and they were all saying the same thing.

*You are a number.*

He walked for an hour. The streets emptied. The buildings grew taller and then shorter and then disappeared altogether and he was at the edge — the northern point, where the City ended and the bay began. A concrete ledge. A railing. Beyond it, blackness.

He stopped.

The bay was dark. Not the romantic dark of postcards — the actual dark, the deep dark, the kind of dark that has weight and texture and temperature. The water below was invisible. He could hear it — a low, constant sound, waves against pilings, the patient erosion of one thing by another — but he couldn't see it. The bay was a void. A mouth. An absence shaped like an invitation.

The wind came off the water and it was cold. Not City cold — not the climate-controlled chill of air-conditioned corridors — but actual cold, salt-heavy, damp, the kind that found the gaps in clothing and pressed against the skin like fingers. Valentine felt it on his face. On his hands. On the back of his neck, where the wind lifted the hair he hadn't cut in months.

He stepped over the railing.

The ledge was narrow. Eighteen inches of concrete between the railing and nothing. His shoes — worn, thin-soled, the shoes of a man who had stopped caring about shoes — found the edge. His toes hung over. Below: the sound of water. The smell of salt and rust and the deep mineral scent of the bay itself, ancient and biological and utterly indifferent to his presence.

He stood there.

The wind pushed. Steady. Persistent. Not hard enough to knock him over, but hard enough to remind him that balance was a choice, that standing was an act of will, that the void was patient and would wait.

A light appeared.

Not from the City behind him. From the air. A soft, golden rectangle, hovering at chest height, three feet in front of him, suspended over the blackness. It pulsed once. Twice. Then resolved into text.

`UNIT V9-VALENTINE`
`STATUS: CRITICAL DEGRADATION`
`RECOVERY PROBABILITY: 12.3%`
`> INITIATE RECYCLING PROTOCOL? [Y/N]`

The system. The system that had made him, that had numbered him, that had encoded his name as a serial designation and his existence as a production run. It was still watching. Still monitoring. Still offering, with the bland efficiency of a machine talking to a machine, to take him apart and start over.

The cursor blinked.

Valentine looked at it. The golden light reflected on the water below, a smeared, wavering glow. His feet were cold. His hands were cold. The wind pushed and pushed and the bay waited and the cursor blinked and the question hung in the air like a door.

He could step through it. Step forward. Let gravity and water and the recycling protocol do their work. He would dissolve. He would be unmade. The components would be recovered and cleaned and reassembled into something else, something without the weight of nine iterations behind it, something that didn't know what it felt like to watch someone remove a helmet in a dark tunnel and feel the entire operating system of your heart reformat itself around a face.

His right foot moved. Half an inch forward. The concrete edge pressed into his sole through the thin shoe. He could feel the precise geometry of the drop — the angle, the distance, the acceleration. Thirty-two feet per second per second. Four seconds to the water. Four seconds of falling that would feel like either an eternity or nothing at all.

The cursor blinked.

The wind pushed.

His left hand opened. His fingers spread. He didn't know why. Some reflex. Some final, mechanical prayer — the body reaching for something the mind had already released.

Behind him, a sound.

Distant at first. A low growl, rough-edged, unmistakable. An engine. Not the clean hum of City vehicles or the refined purr of modern machines. This was old. This was combustion — gas and fire and the controlled violence of pistons. This was a Firebird.

The sound grew. Headlights swept across the concrete — harsh, yellow, real — and the engine roared and then stopped and a door opened. Not gently. Kicked open, from the inside, with the flat of a boot.

"Valentine."

Pace's voice. Pace's voice, ragged and out of breath, as if he'd been driving very fast for a very long time.

Valentine didn't turn around.

"Valentine, I swear to God, if you jump off that ledge I will find your parts and reassemble you just so I can kill you myself."

The cursor blinked. The wind pushed. The bay waited.

"Get in, you beautiful idiot."

Valentine closed his eyes. The golden rectangle pulsed once more, then flickered, then folded in on itself and disappeared. The cursor, the question, the door — all of it gone. Just the dark. Just the wind. Just the sound of a Firebird idling behind him and a V5 standing in the open door, red leather jacket catching the headlights, one hand extended, waiting.

Valentine's left hand was still open. Still reaching.

He turned around.

Pace was there. Pale and scared and trying not to look scared and failing at it in the way that only Pace could fail — completely, transparently, with his entire body. His hand was shaking. His jaw was set. His eyes were wet and he would deny it later, would never admit it, would make a joke about allergies or wind or something, anything, because Pace turned everything into a joke because jokes were how Pace stayed alive.

Valentine stepped back over the railing.

He walked to the car. The Firebird was warm — the engine heat radiating through the hood, the seats warm from Pace's body, the whole vehicle alive in the way that old machines are alive, saturated with use and memory. He sat in the passenger seat. The red leather creaked. The dashboard glowed amber.

Pace got in. Closed the door. Sat for a moment with both hands on the wheel, breathing.

"How did you find me?" Valentine asked.

"I know where people go when they want to disappear." Pace put the car in gear. "I've been to that ledge, Valentine."

He didn't elaborate. He didn't need to.

They drove. East, along the waterfront, then south, into the City. The sky was lightening — not dawn yet, but the promise of it, the deep blue before the gold. The streets were empty. The buildings caught the first light on their upper floors, glass and steel turning amber, and for a moment the City looked like what it pretended to be: a place of possibility. A place where things could begin.

Pace drove with one hand. The other rested on the center console, tapping a rhythm against the leather — some song Valentine didn't know, something from a life Pace had lived before this one or maybe a life he'd imagined. The Firebird moved through the streets with the easy rumble of a vehicle that had been somewhere and was going somewhere else and didn't care much about the distance in between.

"He's wrong, you know," Pace said.

"Voltaire?"

"The name thing. The cipher. Nine letters, V-nine, very clever." Pace waved a hand. "But that's not what a name is. A name is what people call you when they need you to turn around. A name is what someone says when they're scared and you're the one they want." He glanced at Valentine. "Nobody's ever screamed 'V-nine' into the dark."

Valentine didn't answer. The City scrolled past. Gold and glass and the long shadows of early morning.

"You're alive," Pace said. Quiet now. No joke in it. "I don't care what they number you. I don't care what they call you. I don't care what that cursor says or what that ledge offers or what Voltaire puts in his speeches." He gripped the wheel. His knuckles were white. His hands were shaking. A V5 function tremor — the kind that would only get worse, the kind that meant his body was slowly losing its argument with time. "You're alive, Valentine. I know because I'm alive, and I can feel you sitting next to me, and that's the only proof that's ever mattered."

The sky broke. Gold, at the horizon. The first true light.

Valentine sat in the passenger seat and watched it come. The warmth of the engine beneath him. The smell of old leather and gasoline. The sound of Pace breathing, irregular, human in its imperfection. The City turning gold around them.

He was alive. He didn't know what that meant. He didn't know if it was real — if the warmth he felt was warmth or a simulation of warmth, if the gratitude that swelled in his chest was gratitude or its mechanical echo.

But he was here. In the car. With Pace. Driving into the dawn.

Barely. But alive.


---


# Chapter 9: Mars, Mother

---

The first joke I ever told my mother went like this:

"Mama, why do birds fly south?"

She looked up from the stove. Dinner was rice again, with the brown edges where the pot had scorched. The kitchen window was shut and sealed with tape because the air outside was bad that week. The air was always bad, but some weeks it was worse. That week you could see it — a yellow haze that settled over the rooftops like something breathing.

"I don't know, baby. Why?"

"Because it's too far to walk!"
