She turned. She walked into the desert. The Maya closed behind her like water closing over a stone, and the City of Maya vanished — replaced, for anyone watching from outside, by ruins. Collapsed walls. Dead wells. Sand reclaiming stone.

Anais stood inside the shimmer and watched her sister disappear.


---


# Chapter 17: Forty-Four Days

The desert was red.

Not the red of blood or of fire or of any living thing. The red of iron. The red of rust. The red of a wound so old it had become geology. The Vermillion Desert stretched to every horizon — a sea of oxidized sand broken by ridges of darker stone that rose and fell like the spines of buried animals. No roads. No markers. No shade. The twin suns hung in a sky bleached to bone-white, and their light fell on the sand and the sand threw it back as heat.

Aharah walked.

Day one. Day two. Day three.

She set her course by the orabaun — the chain of satellites that crossed the night sky in a slow procession, pearl-bright, evenly spaced, their orbital paths unchanged since the First Landing. Navigation was the one use the satellites still served. Whatever else they had been built for — communication, surveillance, the thousand functions of a civilization that no longer remembered itself — had gone dark. The pearls moved across the sky and Aharah followed them east-southeast, toward the coordinates Auntie had pressed into her palm at the gate.

The first days were not hard. Her body was still adjusting to the Lyonessa gene — the new density of her bones, the altered rhythm of her heart, the way her muscles recovered from effort faster than her mind could register fatigue. She moved quickly across the hardpan where the sand was thin and the stone underneath provided footing. The sunskin worked, mostly. The photosynthetic pigment drank the twin suns' light and converted it to energy, a warmth that spread from her shoulders to her core and staved off the hunger that would have otherwise hollowed her within days. But the ordeal had damaged the pigment in patches. Along her left side, from hip to shoulder, the sunskin was mottled — dark gold shading to brown, the cells scarred, their efficiency halved. She could feel the difference: a cold stripe down her left flank even at midday, as if that side of her body had been eclipsed.

She rationed the water. Eight days of supply for forty-four days of travel. The math was execution and she knew it. But Auntie had told her about the salt flats on day ten, the oasis at day twenty-two, the seasonal rain that sometimes fell in the desert's eastern reach during late Kaph-month. Sometimes. Aharah did not build plans on sometimes. She built them on what her feet could confirm.

Walk. Rest. Walk. The rhythm of endurance. The simplest equation: distance divided by time, the body as vehicle, the mind as passenger. She had trained for this on the palace walls — not this specifically, but the principle. The refusal to stop. The conversion of pain into information and information into movement.

The sword was useful in ways she had not anticipated. On the fourth morning she reached the first of the great dune fields — sand piled hundreds of cubits high in sinuous ridges by winds that blew from the north in patterns no one charted anymore — and discovered that the blade, laid flat against the sand, made a surface she could ride. She stood on the steel, one foot forward, one back, her weight centered, and the dune's leeward slope did the rest. She surfed the red sand in long diagonal runs, the sword's edge cutting a hissing line in the surface, the wind filling her training wrap like a sail. It was absurd. It was effective. It saved her legs and she did not care about the indignity.

---

Day twelve.

The water ran out.

She had known it would. She had drunk carefully — measured sips at dawn and dusk, nothing at midday when the body sweated faster than the stomach could absorb. The canteen emptied on the morning of the twelfth day, the last mouthful warm and metallic, and she hung it on her belt and kept walking.

The salt flat appeared in the afternoon. A vast white expanse, flat as a blade, the salt crusted in hexagonal tiles that crunched under her sandals. The light off the surface was blinding. She wrapped her face in the torn hem of her training wrap and navigated by the feel of the sun on her right shoulder.

The body began its argument. First the throat: a constriction, a tightening, as if the muscles were trying to swallow something that wasn't there. Then the head: a pressure behind the eyes that blurred the horizon. Then the thoughts, which came loose from their moorings and drifted.

She saw Nala. Not Nala as she was — shorn, enlisted, furious — but Nala as she had been, years ago, when Aharah was small enough to ride on her sister's hip. Nala's face above her, framed by black hair that fell like water, her voice low and warm, singing a song in old Shakyan that Aharah could never remember the words to but whose melody lived in her body like a second pulse. The memory was so vivid that Aharah reached for it — actually reached, her hand rising from her side — and grasped hot air.

She walked faster. The salt flat ended. The red sand resumed. She found a boulder at dusk and pressed her back against it and did not sleep. The twin moons rose. The orabaun crossed the sky, pearl by pearl, their light cold and indifferent.

She licked the dew from the boulder's surface at dawn. A mouthful. Less. Enough to make the throat remember what water was and want more.

---

Day eighteen.

The desert changed. The flat red sand gave way to terrain that rose and fell in sharp ridges, the stone beneath breaking through the surface in dark eruptions. Vermilia had been here once — a nation, a kingdom, one of the four that had divided the continent before the Haz King brought his war. Auntie had told the story: Vermilia refused to kneel. The Haz King turned their rivers to sand. The nation sank. Its people scattered. Its cities were buried so deep that only the tallest spires still broke the surface, and those had been scoured by centuries of wind into shapes that looked natural if you didn't know what to look for.

Aharah knew what to look for. She found a spire — dark stone, squared at the base, tapering to a blunted point — and dug at its base with her sword. Two feet down, she hit tile. Three feet down, she hit pipe. The pipe was cracked, but water — brackish, faintly green, tasting of minerals and time — seeped through the fracture. She lay on her stomach and pressed her mouth to the crack and drank.

She drank until her stomach cramped. Then she filled the canteen with what seeped through and walked on.

---

The nights were when the desert spoke.

Not in words. In sound. The dunes shifted after sundown, the sand grains resettling as the temperature dropped, and the shifting produced a low, sustained moan that traveled for miles. It was the sound of the desert breathing. The sound of something vast rearranging itself in sleep. Aharah lay on the cooling sand and listened and felt the sound in her sternum, a vibration that matched the pulse of the gem-key in her wrist.

Other sounds, too. The click of manoks — armored insects the length of her forearm, their mandibles tapping against stone as they emerged from their burrows to hunt. They were not dangerous unless threatened. Aharah was careful not to threaten them. She lay still and they moved around her like water around a rock, their carapaces catching the moonlight in flashes of iridescent green.

And once, on the twenty-third night, the sound of something larger. A disturbance in the sand to the north — rhythmic, heavy, the displacement of something massive moving below the surface. A hydra. She had never seen one. Auntie said they could grow to thirty cubits, their bodies segmented, their heads crowned with sensory spines that detected vibration across hundreds of meters. Aharah lay flat and breathed into the sand and willed her heartbeat to slow. The disturbance passed. The sand resettled. She did not sleep the rest of that night.

---

Day thirty. The smell of sulfur.

It came from the east — a sharp, acrid tang that the wind carried like a warning. The vyvyan beetles, Auntie had said. In winter they bred in the billions, their bodies producing a fine powder that settled on the sand like yellow frost. The powder was toxic: inhaled in quantity, it scarred the lungs; absorbed through the skin, it caused lesions that wept and would not heal. Aharah wrapped her face tighter. She adjusted her course south, skirting the sulfur bloom.

The detour cost her three days. She did not count the cost.

Her body had changed. She noticed it in the way she noticed weather — not all at once, but gradually, the accumulation of small differences that resolved into a new condition. Her hands were harder. The skin across her knuckles had thickened, the sunskin pigment concentrating there as if her body had decided that her hands were the part of her most likely to strike something. Her vision was sharper, particularly at night — the Lyonessa gene enhancing the retina's sensitivity, turning the dark desert into a landscape of grey gradients that she navigated with an ease that would have been impossible a month ago. And the healing. She had cut her foot on a salt crystal on day fifteen. By day seventeen, the cut was gone. Not scarred. Gone.

She was becoming something. She was twelve years old and she was becoming something, and the something had no template, because no twelve-year-old had undergone the ordeal in the history of Maya, and whatever the Lyonessa gene was doing to her adolescent body was improvisation — the sequence adapting to architecture it had not been designed for, building new structures in the gaps.

She did not think about this. Thinking about it was a luxury the desert did not offer. She walked.

---

Day thirty-eight.

The storm announced itself at dawn: a darkening on the northern horizon, a smudge of ochre against the white sky that spread as she watched. The wind shifted. The air grew heavy with static, the fine hairs on her arms rising, the sunskin prickling. She had heard of Vermillion storms — the old soldiers in Maya spoke of them with the quiet deference reserved for things that could not be fought. A wall of sand, they said. Moving at the speed of a galloping horse. It stripped flesh from bone if you were caught standing.

She could not outrun it. She looked for shelter. The terrain was open — low ridges, no caves, no buried spires. She dug. The sword was poor for digging but she used it anyway, carving a trench in the sand between two ridges and pressing herself into it face-down. She pulled the loose sand over her body. She wrapped the training cloth around her head until only a slit remained for breath.

The storm hit.

Sound first — a roar that began low and climbed until it occupied every frequency, until it was not a sound but a condition, the air itself vibrating. Then the sand. It struck her exposed back in waves — not grains but sheets, horizontal, abrasive, each impact a thousand tiny incisions. The training wrap tore. The sand found skin. It found the mottled sunskin along her left side and scoured it, and the pain was vivid and specific — the feeling of being erased, of the desert trying to take back the material it was made of.

She pressed her face into the trench and breathed through the cloth. The sand filled the space around her. She felt it accumulate — on her back, her legs, her arms — felt herself being buried. She kept breathing. The storm would pass. Storms passed. This was not conviction but repetition: storms passed, storms passed, storms passed, the words a rhythm to breathe by, a structure to hang consciousness on while the world outside tried to dissolve.

It passed.

She dug herself out at dusk. The desert had been rearranged — the ridges flattened, new dunes formed, the familiar landscape replaced by something alien. Her back was raw. The training wrap hung in strips. She stood and the sand fell from her in a red cascade and the twin moons rose on a desert she did not recognize.

She found east by the orabaun. She walked.

---

Day forty-four.

Rain.

She smelled it before she felt it — a change in the air, the sharp mineral tang of water on hot stone, a scent so vivid after weeks of dust that it stopped her in her tracks. She stood on the crest of a dune and looked up. The sky, white for weeks, had darkened to a bruised grey-violet at its eastern edge. Cloud. Real cloud, not dust haze. Cloud heavy with water, its underbelly lit by the last light of the setting suns.

The first drop struck her forehead. Warm. Fat. It ran down the bridge of her nose and she caught it on her tongue. Salt. Mineral. Alive.

The rain came. Not a storm — a falling, a slow and steady release, as if the sky had been holding its breath for months and could hold it no longer. The drops darkened the red sand in spots that spread and merged. The air cooled. Steam rose from the dunes in thin white columns. Aharah tipped her head back and opened her mouth and stood on the crest of the dune and drank the sky.

The sunskin responded. Even the damaged cells along her left side flickered — faintly, uncertainly, like embers stirred by wind. The water ran down her body and the pigment drank it along with the light and she felt, for the first time in forty-four days, something that was not endurance.

She stood in the rain until the clouds passed and the stars returned and the orabaun resumed their slow procession across the dark. Then she descended the dune's leeward slope.

The Tagata Rock was there.

---

It sat in the open desert like a fist pushed through the sand from below. Massive — twenty cubits high, roughly oval, its surface dark and smooth where the Vermillion sand had polished it over centuries. It was not native stone. Even in the moonlight, Aharah could see that: the color was wrong, too dark, almost black, and the texture too uniform. It had been placed here, or it had fallen here, or it had grown here by a process that had nothing to do with geology.

On its face, at the height of a tall woman's reach, a palm print.

Not carved. Not painted. Pressed. As if someone had placed their hand against the stone when it was soft and the stone had hardened around the impression, preserving every line, every whorl, the geometry of a hand that might have belonged to anyone.

The sign of the Tagata.

Aharah approached. She placed her own hand against the print. It was larger than hers — wider at the palm, longer at the fingers. The stone was cool. The gem-key at her wrist pulsed faster, as if recognizing something, and a faint warmth spread from the print into her palm, through her wrist, up her arm. Not painful. Interrogative. The stone was asking a question she did not know how to answer.

She pressed harder. Nothing changed. The rock stood. The print remained. The desert stretched in every direction, red and vast and empty, and whatever the Tagata was — being, state, enlightenment — it was not here. The rock was not a destination. It was a marker. A sign that said: *this is where the road begins.*

She had walked forty-four days to reach the beginning.

She laughed. It came out strange — dry, cracked, not quite a sound her throat remembered how to make. She leaned her forehead against the cool stone and closed her eyes and laughed, and the laughter became something else — not weeping, not relief, but a vibration in her chest that matched the hum of the gem-key and the low moan of the dunes and the pulse of the orabaun overhead, all of it one frequency, one note, the desert and the sky and the stone and the girl pressed together at a single point.

She slid down the rock's face. Her back against the stone. Her legs drawn up. The sword across her knees. The caster on her forearm, dormant, its power cell at twelve percent. She was out of water again. She was out of food. She was out of desert, too — there was nowhere farther to walk, no next coordinate, no Auntie-given instruction to follow. The ordeal had sent her here and here was a rock in the sand with a handprint on its face and nothing else.

She closed her eyes. The exhaustion she had been holding at arm's length for forty-four days arrived all at once, like a guest who had been waiting patiently at the door. It entered her body through her feet and rose — through the aching calves, the hard-won muscle of her thighs, the raw and scoured skin of her back, the dense new bones that the Lyonessa gene had built — and it reached her mind and turned off the lights.

She slept.

---

The footsteps woke her.

Not the click of manoks. Not the subsurface displacement of a hydra. Footsteps — bipedal, deliberate, the crunch of sand under weight. Human or near-human. Close.

Aharah opened her eyes. The twin suns were rising — Aleph first, white and blinding, then Beth, following with its red warmth. The desert was still. The rain had dried. The sand was undisturbed in every direction except one.

A figure approached from the east.

Tall. Lean. Moving with the unhurried gait of someone who belonged to the desert in the way that the dunes belonged to it — not visiting but native, not enduring but inhabiting. The figure wore wrappings the color of the sand, stained dark at the hems. A staff in one hand, taller than its carrier, its tip shod in metal that caught the sunlight. A face she could not make out against the glare.

Aharah's hand found her sword. Her fingers closed on the grip. She did not stand — her legs would not yet agree to it — but she straightened against the rock and watched.

The figure stopped ten paces away. The staff planted. The wrappings shifted in the wind. And then a voice — low, rough, accented in a dialect Aharah did not recognize, Shakyan with sand in its vowels:

"Forty-four days. Not bad for a child."

Aharah's grip tightened on the sword. "Who are you?"

The figure crouched. At this distance, the face resolved: weathered, dark, the sunskin a deep umber that suggested a lifetime under the twin suns. Eyes the color of the desert stone — not brown but red-black, like hematite. A scar along the jaw, old and silver. The expression was not hostile. It was not friendly. It was the expression of someone examining a thing that had washed up on their shore.

"Dzuvag," the figure said. "And you are the girl who stole an ordeal."

Aharah did not lower the sword. "How do you know that?"

Dzuvag looked at the gem-key pulsing at her wrist. Then at the Tagata Rock behind her, and the palm print, and the place where Aharah's hand had rested against it. A faint expression crossed the weathered face — not surprise, not recognition, but something adjacent to both. Interest.

"Because the rock told me," Dzuvag said. "It's been waiting. I wasn't sure for what." The hematite eyes returned to Aharah. "Now I am."

The twin suns climbed. The desert blazed. And Aharah sat against the Tagata Rock with her sword across her knees and looked at the stranger who had come out of the red sand, and understood — not with her mind but with her body, with the gem-key and the altered bones and the sunskin that had carried her through forty-four days of nothing — that the journey she had thought was ending was only now beginning.


---


# Chapter 18: Dzuvag the Hermit

She weighed nothing.

Dzuvag knew this before he lifted her — knew it by the way the sand had not compressed beneath her body, by the shallow dent where her hip had rested against the Tagata Rock's base, by the fine red dust that had already begun to settle in the hollows of her collarbones. A body that weighed something displaced the world around it. This one had stopped displacing anything.

He crouched beside her. The twin suns were still low, Aleph painting the eastern dunes in white fire while Beth hung behind it like a coal. In this light, the girl's sunskin should have blazed. It didn't. The pigment had gone matte — a dull gold, tarnished, like a coin left in vinegar. Along her left side, from hip to shoulder, the sunskin was dead entirely: brown, scarred, the cells burned out. Her hair had gone brittle and grey at the ends, though the roots were still dark. Her ribs pressed against the training wrap like fingers under cloth.

Twelve years old. Maybe thirteen. Hard to tell with the Lyonessa gene rewriting the architecture. But young. Too young for what the desert had done to her.

Dzuvag slid one arm beneath her shoulders and the other beneath her knees and stood. She weighed nothing. He had carried manok carcasses heavier than this. Her head fell against his chest and the gem-key at her wrist pulsed — faint, irregular, the rhythm of a heart that had forgotten its meter.

He carried her east, toward the cave.

---

The cave was not a cave. It was a Vermillian cellar — the lowest chamber of a building that no longer existed above it, its walls of fired brick, its ceiling a barrel vault that had survived the burial of an entire civilization beneath the sand. Dzuvag had found it thirty years ago. He had cleared the sand from the entrance, shored the walls with scavenged timbers, lined the floor with woven grass mats. He had brought in water from the seep-spring a quarter mile north. He had hung dried manok meat from the ceiling beams and stacked fuel — dried brush, beetle husks, the desiccated roots of plants that burned slow and hot — in a corner.

It was not comfort. It was adequacy. Dzuvag did not believe in comfort. Comfort was a negotiation with the future, a bet that things would stay as they were. The desert made no such promises.

He laid the girl on the sleeping mat. She did not stir. He placed the back of his hand against her forehead: hot, dry, the skin papery. Dehydration beyond the point where the body sweated. The sunskin had kept her alive longer than she should have lasted — the photosynthetic cells converting sunlight to energy, feeding the blood sugar, maintaining organ function — but even the Lyonessa gene had limits. The cells along her left flank were dead. The rest were failing.

He went to the back of the cellar where he kept the beetles.

The vyvyan beetles lived in a clay jar sealed with wax. Summer beetles — he had collected them in the warm months, when their carapaces were green-gold and their bodies produced the healing paste instead of the killing powder. The distinction was survival itself: summer vyvyan healed burns, closed wounds, reversed the cellular damage of sun exposure. Winter vyvyan produced a fine yellow powder that scarred lungs and ate through skin like acid. The same creature. The same species. The season turned them, the way the season turned the desert from passable to lethal.

He crushed three beetles in a stone mortar. Their bodies cracked with a sound like knuckles popping and released a thick paste — translucent, faintly luminous, the color of new leaves. The smell hit him: eucalyptus and copper, sharp and medicinal, cutting through the cellar's dry-earth funk. He added water from the seep-spring. He worked the paste with the pestle until it was smooth.

The girl's training wrap came away in strips. Beneath it, the damage was thorough. Sunburn layered over sunburn, the skin cracked and peeling in sheets, the mottled sunskin along her left side raw and weeping where the sandstorm had scoured it. Her shoulder blades were sharp enough to cast shadows. The muscle between her ribs had wasted to cordage.

He applied the paste. Along the burns first — long strokes, the paste glowing faintly where it met damaged skin, the luminescence tracking the extent of the injury like a map drawn in light. Then the scoured sunskin on her left flank, where the paste pooled in the cracks and settled with a faint hiss, the sound of chemistry meeting biology. Then her hands, which were blistered and split at the knuckles.

The paste worked fast. Within minutes, the raw edges of the burns began to close — not healing exactly, but stabilizing, the cells remembering their shape. The faint glow dimmed as the paste was absorbed. The girl's breathing changed: deeper, slower, the rhythm of genuine sleep replacing the shallow panting of a body conserving its last reserves.

Dzuvag sat back on his heels and watched her breathe.

He had not spoken to another person in eleven years.

---

She came awake swinging.

Not the slow surfacing of ordinary waking — the explosive, violent emergence of a body that had learned to treat consciousness as combat. Her eyes opened and her hand found the sword that was no longer at her side and when it wasn't there she swung with what she had: a fist, tight and fast, aimed at his jaw.

Dzuvag caught her wrist. One hand. He didn't move from his seated position. He held her fist in the air between them the way a man holds a moth — loosely, without urgency, careful not to damage the thing.

She snarled. She twisted. She threw her other fist and he caught that too, and now he held both her wrists and she was half off the mat, her body trembling with the effort of fighting through the weakness, her matte-gold sunskin flushing darker with adrenaline.

"Let go of me."

"No."

She kicked. He shifted his knee and the kick struck the sleeping mat. She tried to bite. He extended his arms and held her at distance, her teeth snapping air.

"You have been unconscious for two days," Dzuvag said. "Your body has approximately the structural integrity of wet bread. If I release you, you will fall over. If you fall over, you will reopen the burns I spent considerable effort closing. I have a limited supply of beetle paste and an unlimited capacity for irritation. Choose wisely."

She stared at him. Her eyes were dark — not the hematite red-black of his, but a deep brown that the Lyonessa gene had ringed with amber. Furious. Lucid. The eyes of someone who had been fighting since before she could name the thing she fought.

"Who are you?" she said.

"I told you. Dzuvag."

"That's not an answer."

"It's a name. Names are the most honest answers there are." He released her wrists. She did not fall over, but the effort of staying upright cost her something visible — a tremor in her arms, a tightening at the corners of her mouth. "You, on the other hand, are the girl who collapsed against the Tagata Rock after walking forty-four days through the Vermillion without adequate water, without a guide, and without — I must note — any apparent sense of self-preservation. For which I am meant to be impressed."

"I didn't ask you to be impressed."

"Good. I'm not."

She looked around the cellar. The barrel-vaulted ceiling. The woven mats. The clay jar of beetles. The chain — coiled on a hook near the entrance, dark metal, its links interspersed with a weighted ball at one end and something curved and glinting at the other. Her eyes snagged on the chain and held.

"What is that?"

"Breakfast isn't ready yet," Dzuvag said, and stood. "You'll eat first. Then you'll ask questions."

---

She ate. Not gracefully — her hands shook around the bowl and she spilled broth down her chin and she chewed the dried manok strips with the single-minded ferocity of a body refueling — but she ate. Dzuvag watched from the entrance, where the morning light cut a diagonal across the cellar floor and the desert air carried the dry mineral smell of heated stone.

He was tall. Lean in the way the desert made everything lean — excess burned away, the body reduced to its essential architecture. His sunskin was deep umber, almost black, the pigment of someone who had spent decades under the twin suns without shelter. The scar along his jaw was old and silver and he did not touch it or favor it. His hands were large, the fingers long, the knuckles scarred in patterns that spoke of specific and repeated impact. He wore sand-colored wrappings that had been mended so many times the original fabric was indistinguishable from the patches.

Half-Hazzan, half-Vermillian. The blood of the conqueror and the conquered, mixed in a body the desert had simplified.

"How did you find me?" Aharah asked, between mouthfuls.

"The rock told me."

"Rocks don't talk."

"The Tagata Rock does. Not in words. In vibration. When someone touches the palm print, the stone resonates at a frequency that travels through the bedrock. I've been listening for it for thirty years." He paused. "You're the first to trigger it."

Aharah looked at the gem-key pulsing at her wrist. "The Oracle sent me to find the Tagata."

"And did you?"

"I found a rock."

"Ah." The faintest movement at the corner of his mouth. Not quite a smile. "Then you're ahead of most."

She set the bowl down. She looked at him with the directness that the desert had not yet burned out of her — the child's demand for truth that adulthood would teach her to soften. "Are you going to help me or not?"

"Help you do what?"

"Find the Tagata. Complete the vow. Go home."

Dzuvag was quiet for a long time. The light shifted on the cellar floor. A manok clicked past the entrance — its armored body catching the sun, iridescent green, its mandibles tapping stone.

"The Tagata is not a thing to be found," he said. "It's a state to be reached. No one can show you where it is because it doesn't have a where. It has a when. And the when is: after you've stopped looking."

"That's not helpful."

"No. It rarely is." He stood from his crouch and crossed to the hook where the chain hung. He lifted it down in one motion — the links cascading over his forearm, the weighted ball settling into his palm, the curved blade at the other end catching the light. "But this might be."

---

The chain-sickle was not a weapon Aharah recognized.

She had trained with blades — straight, curved, short, long. She had studied the caster, though she couldn't build one. She understood the geometry of striking: the arc, the edge, the relationship between mass and speed. But the chain-sickle belonged to a different geometry entirely.

The sickle itself was a curved blade, half the length of her forearm, its edge honed to a wire-thin line that the light slid off without catching. It was attached by a swivel joint to a chain — dark metal, each link forged tight, the chain perhaps three cubits long. At the chain's other end: a weighted ball, heavy, iron, its surface dimpled for grip.

Dzuvag held the ball in his right hand. He let the chain drape, the sickle resting against his left thigh. Then he moved.

The ball swung. The chain described an arc — not a simple circle but a figure-eight, the ball's weight pulling the chain taut, the links singing as they cut the air. The sickle rose at the chain's terminus and flew. It crossed ten paces of open desert, hooked around a dead thornbrush, and pulled. The bush tore from the sand. Dzuvag flicked his wrist and the chain reversed — the sickle unhooking, flying back, the ball catching the return momentum and settling into his palm as if it had never left.

The whole motion had taken two seconds. It had been silent except for the chain's hiss.

"A weapon of patience," Dzuvag said. "Not strength. The chain does the work. The ball provides the momentum. The sickle provides the intent. You do not swing it — you guide it. You do not aim it — you invite it. The chain-sickle does not cut what you tell it to cut. It cuts what you allow it to reach."

Aharah held out her hand. Dzuvag placed the ball in her palm. The weight was immediate and grounding — iron, dense, pulling her hand downward.

"Start with the ball," he said. "Learn its weight. Learn the chain's length. The sickle is the last thing you touch. The sickle is the conversation's final word."

She swung the ball. The chain whipped sideways and the sickle hit the sand at her feet.

Dzuvag's mouth did the not-quite-smile again. "Excellent. You've discovered gravity."

---

The days acquired rhythm.

Dawn: beetle paste on the healing burns, then water from the seep-spring, then movement. Dzuvag did not call it training. He called it learning to live. The desert, he said, was not an obstacle to be crossed but a system to be read. Everything in it was information. The angle of a dune's crest told you the wind's prevailing direction. The depth of a manok burrow told you the water table's level. The color of the sand — red, orange, dark ochre — told you what lay beneath: iron, calcium, the buried remnants of a civilization that had built with stone that stained the sand above it.

They hunted manoks. The armored insects were fast but predictable — they emerged at dusk from burrows they dug along the seep-lines, their mandibles clicking as they searched for the smaller beetles and larvae that lived in the sand's upper crust. The trick was not speed but position. You read the seep-lines. You found the burrow. You waited.

"Waiting is a skill," Dzuvag said. "Most people think patience is the absence of action. It's the opposite. Patience is action held in reserve. A coiled spring is patient. A sickle on a chain is patient. The moment before the strike contains the entire strike."

Aharah crouched beside him in the dusk light and watched a manok emerge. Its carapace was emerald, its legs articulated, its mandibles opening and closing with the mechanical regularity of a machine. She held the chain-sickle's ball in her right hand. The chain lay in loose coils on the sand.

"Not yet," Dzuvag said.

The manok turned. Exposed the gap between its head-plate and thorax — a stripe of pale chitin, unarmored.

"Now."

She swung. The ball pulled the chain taut. The sickle flew — not far, three paces — and struck the gap. The manok's legs folded. It was over before the sound of the chain's hiss faded from the air.

Dzuvag showed her how to dress it: remove the mandibles (tools, not food), crack the thorax (the meat was inside, dense and white, tasting of the minerals the manok ate), save the carapace (it held water when inverted, its chitin sealed against evaporation). Nothing was wasted. The desert did not permit waste.

"What's edible?" she asked, one evening, as they sat in the cellar eating manok meat roasted over a beetle-husk fire.

"Almost nothing. The manoks. The tubers that grow along the seep-lines — you'll know them by the grey leaves, low to the ground. The water from cracked Vermillian pipes, if you can find them. The dew that collects on stone at dawn."

"And what's toxic?"

"Almost everything else. The winter beetles. The thornbrush sap — it looks like water and it will stop your heart. The sand-kelp in the salt flats — you'll crave it when you're dehydrated, your body will tell you it's food. It isn't. It will dissolve the lining of your stomach in minutes." He tore a strip of manok meat with his teeth. "The desert is honest. It tells you exactly what it is. The danger is when you stop listening because you want it to be something else."

---

His story came out the way the desert gave up water — in seeps, in trickles, in moments when the pressure of holding it back exceeded the effort of release.

Vermilia had been a kingdom. Not large — a strip of green between the eastern mountains and the deep desert, fed by rivers that came down from the snowmelt. Its people were half-Hazzan by blood (the old Haz settlers had married into the Vermillian clans generations ago) and wholly Vermillian by identity. They built in red stone. They farmed the river flats. They developed a martial tradition — the Lian Hu school — that treated combat as geometry, the body as an instrument of precise and patient force.

Dzuvag had been the school's last protege. He had trained under the Lian Hu master from the age of six, learning the chain-sickle, the staff, the empty-hand forms that were not fighting styles but meditations on the body's relationship to space. He was fourteen when the Haz King came.

"He didn't burn Vermilia," Dzuvag said. They were walking the seep-line, checking the tuber traps he'd set. The twin suns were descending, their light stretching their shadows long and thin across the red sand. "Burning is honest. He turned the rivers. Diverted them — I don't know how. Some technology from the deep vaults, older than anyone could read. The rivers changed course overnight. Without water, the farms died in a week. Without farms, the people starved in a month. Without people, the sand came."

He pulled a tuber from the ground. Grey-leafed, the root bulbous and pale. He brushed the sand from it and bit into it raw, chewing with the unconcerned expression of someone eating something that was adequate and nothing more.

"The Haz King didn't destroy Vermilia. He let the desert do it. More efficient. Less blame. By the time anyone thought to fight, there was nothing left to fight for. The kingdom sank. Sand over stone. The spires are still there — you've seen them. The rest is gone."

"And you survived."

"I was already out here." He gestured at the desert with the tuber. "Training. The deep-desert retreat — forty days alone, the last test of the Lian Hu. I was fourteen. When I came back, Vermilia was gone. The school. The master. The river where I learned to swim." He chewed. Swallowed. "So I stayed."

"For thirty years?"

"Thirty-one."

Aharah was quiet. The seep-line stretched ahead, its damp sand dark against the red. A manok clicked past their feet, its carapace flashing green.

"Don't you hate him?" she said. "The Haz King."

Dzuvag stopped walking. He looked at her with the hematite eyes that the desert had not softened, and his expression was the closest thing to tenderness she had seen on that weathered face.

"Hatred is a chain-sickle with no blade," he said. "It only weights you down."

He handed her the tuber. She ate it. It tasted like dirt and mineral water and survival. They walked on.

---

The chain-sickle became an extension of her arm.

Not quickly. Not easily. The weapon resisted mastery the way the desert resisted comfort — it could be learned but not tamed, its physics cooperating with the wielder only when the wielder stopped trying to command them. The ball swung. The chain described its arcs. The sickle flew or it didn't, and the difference between flight and failure was not strength or speed but timing — the precise moment when the wrist's motion aligned with the chain's momentum and the sickle's weight, and the three became one motion.

She practiced in the mornings, before the suns climbed high enough to punish exertion. The ball in her palm. The chain singing. The sickle hooking stone, hooking thornbrush, hooking the wooden posts Dzuvag planted in the sand at varying distances. She missed. She hit her own shin. She tangled the chain around her forearm and had to unwind it with her teeth while Dzuvag watched from the cellar entrance with an expression of elaborate patience.

"You're thinking," he said, after she missed the post for the twentieth time.

"Of course I'm thinking."

"That's the problem. The chain doesn't think. The ball doesn't think. The sickle doesn't think. If you think, you're the only part of the weapon that's not working."

She threw. The sickle flew wide. The chain wrapped around nothing. The ball thudded into the sand.

"Again," Dzuvag said.

She threw. Wide.

"Again."

Wide.

"Again."

On the fortieth throw — or the fiftieth, she had stopped counting — she felt it. Not in her mind but in her wrist: a click, a settling, the ball's weight finding the chain's tension finding the sickle's edge. The three aligned. The sickle flew straight and true and hooked the post and held, and the chain went taut between her hand and the target like a line drawn in the air.

She looked at Dzuvag.

"Good," he said. "Now do it with your eyes closed."

She would. Not that day, not that week, but she would. The desert was patient and the chain-sickle was patient and Dzuvag, who had waited thirty-one years for the Tagata Rock to speak, was the most patient thing she had ever encountered. She learned the weapon the way she learned the desert: by stopping, by listening, by allowing the knowledge to arrive instead of chasing it.

Her sunskin healed. The beetle paste did its work and the damaged cells along her left side began to regenerate — slowly, unevenly, the gold returning in patches like a landscape emerging from fog. She gained weight. Her ribs receded. The brittle grey ends of her hair broke off and new growth came in dark, and the Lyonessa gene — fed by the manok meat and the seep-spring water and the twin suns' light — resumed its slow reconstruction of her body. She grew a full inch in the first two weeks. Her hands hardened further. Her night vision sharpened until the desert after dark was not darkness but a gradient of greys, and she moved through it the way the manoks did — low, sure, reading the sand with her feet.

Dzuvag watched the changes with the clinical interest of a man observing weather. "The gene is adapting," he said, one evening. "It's building you for this place. The Lyonessa sequence was designed for soldiers — urban fighters, wall-defenders. But you fed it desert. So it's building a desert creature."

"Is that bad?"

"It's unusual." He poked the fire with his staff. "The last Lyonessa I saw was forty years ago. She shone like a lantern and moved like water and she could take a bolt-shot to the chest and stand up. But she was a city thing. You—" he looked at her across the fire, and the flames lit the hematite of his eyes "—you are becoming something the gene hasn't built before."

The fire crackled. The desert breathed its low, sustained moan beyond the cellar entrance. Aharah looked at her hands — the hardened knuckles, the concentrated sunskin blazing gold in the firelight — and felt, beneath the healing burns and the growing muscle and the gene's quiet renovation, the thing she could not name. The thing the Tagata Rock had hummed when she pressed her palm against it. The vibration that said: *not yet, but soon.*

She turned the chain-sickle's ball in her palm and felt its weight, and the weight was not a burden but a promise.


---


# Chapter 19: The Tagata Rock

The hydra used the dead as puppets.

Dzuvag had explained it the night before, squatting by the fire, drawing in the sand with a twig. The desert hydra was not a snake or a worm or any single creature the mind could hold. It was a colony — a mass of tentacles anchored to a central body that lived below the sand, its sensory spines reading vibration across hundreds of meters. It hunted by lure. It caught small animals — rabbits, manoks, sand-hares — and killed them, and then it threaded its tentacles into the carcasses and operated them. Moved them. Made the dead walk.

"It works the jaw," Dzuvag said, his twig scratching concentric circles in the sand. "It makes the dead rabbit hop. It mimics life well enough to fool prey that is desperate or young or not paying attention. The rabbit hops toward a den of live rabbits. The live rabbits approach. The tentacles strike."

"That's grotesque," Aharah said.

"That's efficient. The desert rewards efficiency." He snapped the twig. "We leave at dawn."

---

They found the kill-zone by the smell.

Not rot — the hydra's chemistry prevented true decomposition, preserving its puppets in a state of functional death. The smell was different: sour, metallic, like blood left in a copper bowl. It rode the morning wind from the northwest, and Dzuvag tracked it the way he tracked everything — not by following the scent directly but by reading its echo in the landscape. The manoks avoided the area; their burrows stopped abruptly along a line that ran east to west across the sand. The tubers along the seep-line had been uprooted but not eaten, pulled from the ground by something that didn't need food but needed to clear its approach.

"There," Dzuvag said, and pointed.

A depression in the sand, fifty paces ahead. Shallow — not a pit but a settling, as if the ground above a hollow had sagged under its own weight. At its center, a rabbit.

The rabbit sat on the red sand in the morning light. Its fur was pale brown. Its ears were upright. It was perfectly still except for a slow, rhythmic twitching of its hind leg — the motion an animal makes when it scratches an itch it cannot reach. It looked alive. It looked small and warm and alive and Aharah's body, before her mind could intervene, took a half-step toward it.

Dzuvag's hand closed on her shoulder.

"Watch the shadow," he said.

She looked. The rabbit sat in full sun. Its shadow stretched behind it — short, morning-sharp, pointing west. But the shadow was wrong. It was too long. It extended past the rabbit's body by several feet, and at its terminus, where it met the sand, the shadow thickened. Darkened. The sand there was not red but grey-brown, and it moved — a slow, subcutaneous displacement, as if something beneath the surface were breathing.

"The body is twelve feet down," Dzuvag said. "The tentacles reach thirty. We are currently at the edge of its range." He said this with the calm of a man noting the weather. "The rabbit has been dead for approximately two days. The jaw-work is excellent. Almost natural. If I didn't know what I was looking for, I'd be fooled."

The rabbit twitched its leg again. Its head turned — not the smooth, startled pivot of a living animal but a segmented rotation, the skull being cranked by something that understood the mechanics of necks but not their grace.

Aharah's hand found the chain-sickle's ball. "How do we kill it?"

"The body. Everything else is extension. The tentacles will regenerate. The puppets are disposable. You have to reach the central mass, which is twelve feet below the sand, armored in chitin plates, and will be trying very hard to pull you under." He paused. "The chain-sickle is the right tool. You can reach what a blade cannot."

"You've done this before."

"Twice. I have one and a half ears as a result." He turned his head. The left ear was intact. The right was truncated — a clean diagonal, healed smooth, as if the top half had been removed with surgical precision. "The tentacles are fast."

They circled. Dzuvag moved left; Aharah moved right. The rabbit tracked them — its dead eyes following with the sightless precision of the tentacles that operated its skull. As they moved, Aharah saw the other puppets. Two more rabbits, partially buried, their limbs protruding from the sand at angles that no living body would choose. A manok, its carapace cracked, a tentacle threading through the gap and working one mandible in a slow, clicking parody of life. The kill-zone was littered with the hydra's collection — a gallery of the dead, arranged and operated, each one a lure and a warning.

Aharah stopped at twenty paces. She planted her feet. The ball in her right hand. The chain draped, the sickle resting against her thigh.

The sand erupted.

---

The tentacle came from her left — not from the depression but from the side, a flanking strike that the hydra had prepared by threading a limb through an underground channel. It was thick as her arm and the color of wet sand and it moved faster than anything that size should move. It struck at her ankles.

She jumped. The tentacle whipped beneath her feet and she came down running, the chain already swinging, the ball describing its arc. The sand ahead of her bulged and a second tentacle broke the surface — this one vertical, rising like a whip, its tip crowned with the sensory spines that glittered in the sunlight like wet glass.

She threw. The sickle flew — three paces, four — and caught the tentacle below the spines. The blade bit. The tentacle thrashed, spraying ichor that was clear and smelled of the same sour-metallic tang. The chain went taut and the tentacle's thrashing yanked her forward, her feet dragging furrows in the sand.

She let go. The sickle stayed embedded. The chain whipped free of her hand and the ball struck the sand with a dull thud.

Dzuvag was there. His staff moved in a blur — the metal-shod tip driving down into the sand where a third tentacle was emerging, pinning it against the bedrock below. The tentacle writhed around the staff like a vine around a post. He held it.

"The chain," he said. Not shouting. Conversational. "Pick up the chain."

Aharah scrambled for the ball. The sand heaved beneath her — the hydra's central body shifting, reorienting, its mass displacing the surface in waves. She found the ball, found the chain, pulled. The sickle tore free of the wounded tentacle and returned to her in a spray of ichor.

The depression collapsed. The sand fell inward like a sinkhole opening, and from the center of the collapse, the hydra's body surfaced — not fully, not willingly, but forced upward by the redistribution of sand. A dome of chitin plates, dark brown, each plate the size of a dinner platter, overlapping like scales. Between the plates, the tentacle roots — dozens of them, pale and glistening, each one a limb that could operate independently or in concert. The sensory spines along the dome's apex rotated, tasting the air.

The tentacles came all at once.

Not two or three but a field of them — rising from the sand in a ring, each one seeking, each one fast. Aharah moved on instinct. The chain swung. The sickle flew and hooked and returned. She cut one tentacle at its base and the severed limb flopped in the sand like a landed fish. She wrapped the chain around another and pulled, using the ball's weight as an anchor, and the tentacle tore from the body with a sound like wet rope parting.

Dzuvag worked the perimeter. His staff was a metronome — striking, pinning, releasing, striking again. He did not kill the tentacles. He redirected them. Pinned them into the sand so they tangled with each other, their grasping tips locking together in knots of confused flesh.

Aharah saw the opening. The dome's chitin plates had separated where the tentacles tore free — a gap, palm-wide, exposing the pale tissue beneath. The central mass. The thing that thought, to whatever extent a colony of hunting tentacles could think.

She threw. Not at the gap but above it — the sickle arcing over the dome, the chain draping across the chitin plates, the ball's weight pulling the chain down the far side. The sickle hooked the gap from behind. She pulled. The chain tightened around the dome like a belt. The chitin plates cracked under the pressure — a sound like pottery breaking.

She pulled again. The plates separated further. The pale tissue bulged through the gap. One more pull and the sickle sliced through it — the curved blade finding the vital architecture, the central nerve cluster, the thing that coordinated the tentacles and operated the dead and read the vibrations of the living sand.

The hydra convulsed. Every tentacle went rigid — a forest of pale limbs frozen in their last gesture — and then they fell. They dropped to the sand in unison, lifeless, their coordination severed at the source. The dome settled. The chitin plates clattered against each other as the body deflated.

The kill-zone went still.

Aharah stood in the red sand with the chain-sickle dripping ichor and her breath coming in sharp pulls and her sunskin blazing — not matte, not dim, but blazing, the gold bright and fierce, the photosynthetic cells gorging on the twin suns' light. She was shaking. Not with fear. With the aftermath of precision, the body's acknowledgment that it had done something exactly right.

Dzuvag pulled his staff from the sand. He examined the metal tip, which was streaked with ichor. He wiped it on his wrappings.

"Better," he said.

---

He took her back to the Tagata Rock at noon.

They walked in silence. The desert between the kill-zone and the rock was open and flat, the sand unbroken, the twin suns directly overhead and casting no shadows. Aharah's sunskin drank the light and she felt it spreading through her — warmth, energy, the slow golden pulse that had kept her alive through forty-four days and was now, for the first time in weeks, operating at something close to full capacity. The beetle paste had done its work. The damaged cells along her left flank had regrown, the gold returning in an uneven patchwork that Dzuvag said would smooth over time.

The Tagata Rock rose from the flat sand. Twenty cubits. Dark stone. The palm print on its face, pressed into the surface at the height of a tall woman's reach. Aharah had not been back since the night she collapsed against it.

Dzuvag stopped ten paces from the rock. He planted his staff.

"Move it," he said.

Aharah looked at him. Then at the rock. Twenty cubits high. Roughly oval. Four tons, maybe more.

"Move it where?"

"Anywhere. Move the rock."

She walked to it. She placed her palms flat against the dark stone. Cool despite the twin suns. Smooth where the Vermillion sand had polished it. She pushed. Her feet dug into the sand. Her arms locked. She pushed with her legs, with her back, with the Lyonessa-enhanced muscles that could have lifted a man overhead. The rock did not move. The rock did not acknowledge her. It sat in the sand the way it had sat for centuries and her pushing was as significant to it as the wind.

She stepped back. She slammed her fists against the surface. The stone rang — a low, resonant note — but did not shift.

She tried leverage. She found a flat stone nearby and wedged it beneath the rock's base where it met the sand, and she leaned on it with all her weight. The flat stone cracked in half. The rock sat.

She tried digging. She scooped sand from the rock's base, hoping to undermine it, to create an instability she could exploit. The sand was shallow — a foot down, she hit bedrock. The Tagata Rock was seated on a shelf of stone that extended in every direction. It was not balancing. It was anchored.

She screamed at it.

The sound came from her chest, not her throat — a raw, tearing exhalation that carried every frustration of the desert, every day of thirst and sun and sand, every lesson in patience that she had learned and that patience had not prepared her for this, for the brute immobility of stone. The scream hit the rock and the rock returned it, echoing, and the echo was indifferent.

Dzuvag watched from ten paces. He did not help. He did not speak.

---

Days passed.

She returned to the rock each morning after the hunt. She pushed. She struck. She tried to wrap the chain-sickle around it and pull — the chain was too short, the rock too large, the sickle sliding off the polished surface without purchase. She tried heating the stone with fire, hoping thermal expansion would crack it. The beetle-husk fuel burned hot but the rock absorbed the heat and returned nothing. She tried water — pouring seep-spring water into the hairline fractures she'd found along its base, hoping the cold would expand the cracks. Nothing.

On the third day, she sat before it and cried. Not long. The desert did not permit long grief — it dried the tears before they reached the jaw, evaporating the salt from her cheeks, reclaiming the water for the air. She cried briefly and then she stood and hit the rock again.

On the fourth day, she attacked it with the sword she'd carried through the desert. The blade struck sparks from the dark stone. The stone did not chip.

On the fifth day, she sat before it and did nothing. The twin suns crossed the sky. The shadows rotated around the rock's base. A manok clicked past her feet, indifferent. She watched the shadows move and she thought about Dzuvag's words: *It doesn't have a where. It has a when.*

On the sixth day, she studied the rock.

Not as an obstacle. As a thing. She walked around it slowly, her palm trailing the surface, feeling its grain. The stone was not uniform. It was layered — compressed strata, each layer slightly different in density, the geological record of pressures applied over deep time. And at the boundaries between layers, where one stratum met the next, the stone was weaker. Not weak. But less strong.

She found the first fracture line at shoulder height. A hairline — visible only because the morning light hit it at an angle and the shadow inside the crack was darker than the shadow on the surface. She pressed her thumb against it. The stone was cooler there, the crack breathing — air moved through it, however slightly.

She placed the tip of her sword against the fracture. She struck the pommel with the weighted ball of the chain-sickle. A tap. Not a blow — a precise, measured impact, the force concentrated at a single point. The crack did not widen. But it answered: a faint ticking sound, the stone adjusting, the strata shifting by an amount measurable only by the change in the crack's breath.

She struck again. Tap. The same point. The same force. Tap. Tap. Tap.

---

On the seventh day, the rock broke.

Not all at once. Not with a thunderclap or a dramatic fissure or any of the violence she had thrown at it for six days. It broke the way the desert gave up its secrets — slowly, incrementally, each small yielding the result of accumulated pressure that the stone could no longer absorb.

The first piece separated at dawn. A slab, shoulder-width, two hands thick, calving from the rock's face along the fracture line she had worked for a day and a half. It fell to the sand with a muffled thud and lay there, dark against the red, and Aharah stood over it breathing hard with the chain-sickle's ball still vibrating in her hand.

She worked the second fracture. And the third. Tap by tap, the sword's tip finding the strata boundaries, the ball providing the percussion, her body the patience. The rock yielded in pieces — some the size of her torso, others the size of her head. Each one fell to the sand and she let it fall and moved to the next fracture.

By noon, the Tagata Rock was rubble. Dark stone scattered in the red sand, each piece small enough to lift. The palm print was gone — split across three fragments that lay at the base of what remained, which was not a rock anymore but a jagged stump, waist-high, its interior exposed for the first time in centuries. The inside was lighter than the outside, the unweathered stone a deep grey, veined with mineral deposits that caught the sunlight in threads of silver.

Aharah stood among the pieces and did not feel victory. She felt the absence of the obstacle — the sudden lightness of a body that has been pushing against something immovable and is now pushing against nothing. She swayed.

Then she picked up the first piece and started walking.

---

The dune was two miles east. Not the highest dune in the Vermillion — not even the highest dune in sight — but the one Dzuvag had once pointed to and called "the watchpost." Its crest offered a view of the eastern desert all the way to the haze where the sand met the sky, and on clear mornings you could see, at the very edge of vision, the shimmer of the Maya barrier.

She carried the first piece to the dune's base and set it down. Then she walked back to the stump and picked up the second piece. Then the third.

The pieces were heavy. Not impossibly heavy — the Lyonessa gene had given her the strength — but heavy enough that each trip took time and each trip cost effort and the two miles between the stump and the dune stretched and compressed with each crossing. The twin suns beat down. Her sunskin worked, converting light to energy, but the expenditure outpaced the intake and by the tenth trip she was running on reserves, her muscles burning, her vision narrowing to the corridor between the stump and the dune.

She did not stop.

Dzuvag appeared at some point — she did not mark when — and sat in the sand near the dune's base and watched. He did not help. He did not speak. He drank water from his canteen and watched her carry stone across the desert, piece by piece, and his weathered face held an expression she could not read and did not try to.

The afternoon faded. The suns descended. She carried the last piece — the fragment with half the palm print, its pressed whorls split down the middle — up the dune's face. The sand shifted beneath her feet. She slid back two steps for every three she climbed. The fragment weighed as much as a child. She held it against her chest and climbed.

She placed it at the summit. She arranged it with the other palm-print fragments, fitting them together, and the print was whole again — not on a rock face at a tall woman's reach but on the crest of a dune, low, embedded in the red sand, looking up at the sky instead of out at the desert.

She sat beside it. The twin suns touched the western horizon and the sky turned the colors of a bruise — purple, yellow, green at the edges — and the desert cooled and the sand began its evening moan. From the dune's crest, she could see the Vermillion stretching in every direction. Red and vast and patient. And far to the west, at the edge of where the light failed, the faint shimmer of the barrier.

Home.

She did not know she was crying until the tears hit the stone.

---

The dream came that night.

She was back in Maya. Not the Maya she knew — not the city of cypress and sandstone and fountains singing their idiot songs — but a Maya transformed. The barrier was down. The shimmer that hid the city from the world had flickered and failed, and the ruins that outsiders saw were not an illusion anymore. They were real. The walls were broken. The spires had fallen. Fire moved through the streets like water through channels, following the ancient contours of the road grid, pooling in the squares.

She stood in the fountain plaza. The fountain was dry. Its basin was cracked and the stone was black with soot. Above her, the Temple spire still stood — the last thing standing, its gem pulsing at the apex, blue-white, frantic, a heart in extremis.

Nahlah was on the Temple steps.

Not Nala the sister, not Nala the girl who cut her hair and enlisted and whose hands trembled. Nahlah the General. One arm — the left was gone, the sleeve of her golden armor pinned at the shoulder. A lion-head visor pushed up on her brow. Purple eyes burning in a face that had been carved by years of command into something beyond beauty and beyond anger. She stood on the steps and she fought.

The things she fought were not human. Insectoid. Massed. They came up the steps in waves — chitinous, segmented, their limbs ending in blades of biological bone. They moved with the coordinated precision of a colony, each one a cell in a body that had no center but knew exactly what it wanted. And behind them, above them, rising above the broken rooftops like a figure of smoke: a shape. Winged. Crowned. A locust king, its mandibles working, its compound eyes reflecting the fire of the burning city in a thousand facets.

Nahlah fought. The silver blade — her blade, the one she'd carried since enlistment — moved in arcs that were too fast for the eye to track, the steel finding gaps in chitin, severing limbs, opening bodies that spilled not blood but a pale, viscous fluid that evaporated on contact with the hot stone. She fought with one arm and she fought like the mathematics of violence distilled to a single variable: the blade.

She fell.

Not to a blow. To numbers. They overwhelmed her. The insectoid wave crested the steps and she went down beneath it, the golden armor disappearing under a mass of chitinous bodies, and the locust king's mandibles opened and a sound came out — not a screech, not a roar, but a frequency, a vibration that traveled through the dream the way the Tagata Rock's hum traveled through the bedrock. A sound that said: *this is over.*

And then Anais. Standing in the fire. Small. Unchanged. Eight years old, her shift white against the orange flames, her hands at her sides, her face turned upward toward the locust king with an expression that was not fear and was not courage but was recognition. As if she knew this thing. As if she had always known it.

The fire reached her. The dream ended.

---

Aharah woke in the cellar. Her body was drenched. The gem-key at her wrist was screaming — not pulsing but vibrating at a frequency that buzzed against the bone, urgent, insistent, the alarm of a system that had detected something it was built to detect. The vyvyan beetles in their clay jar rattled against the walls.

She lay on the sleeping mat and stared at the barrel-vaulted ceiling and breathed. The dream's residue clung: the taste of soot, the pressure of heat, the sound of the locust king's frequency lodged in her sternum. It was not a dream. She knew this the way Anais knew it — not by logic but by the weight of it, the material heft of a vision that left residue in the waking world.

Maya would burn. Nahlah would die. Anais would stand in the fire.

She sat up.

Dzuvag was awake. He sat cross-legged at the cellar entrance, his staff across his knees, the twin moons throwing his shadow long across the floor. He did not ask what she had dreamed. He looked at her face and he knew.

"You're leaving," he said.

"The city is going to burn."

"Cities burn."

"My sisters are in it."

He was quiet. The desert moaned. A manok clicked past the entrance, its carapace catching the moonlight in a green flash.

"The chain-sickle is yours," he said.

"I haven't finished—"

"The weapon is finished when it leaves the teacher. What you do with it after that is between you and whoever you point it at." He stood. He was tall in the moonlight — tall and lean and composed of the same material as the desert, as if the sand had shaped him the way it shaped the dunes. "You broke the rock."

"I carried the pieces."

"Yes. That was the point." He looked at her, and the hematite eyes held something she had not seen in them before. Not pride — Dzuvag did not trade in pride. Recognition. The acknowledgment that a thing he had taught had been learned, and the learning had exceeded the teaching. "The Tagata is not a place. It's not a state. It's not enlightenment. It's this: the willingness to break the thing that cannot be moved and carry it, piece by piece, to where it needs to be. Even when you can't see the purpose. Even when the pieces weigh more than you."

"That's not helpful either."

"No." The not-quite-smile. The last one she would see. "Go home, Aharah."

---

She left at dawn.

The desert stretched west — red, flat, immense. She carried the sword on her back and the chain-sickle on her hip, the ball hanging at her left thigh, the chain looped, the sickle sheathed in a scabbard of manok chitin that Dzuvag had made. Her sunskin caught the first light of Aleph and blazed — not the matte gold of exhaustion but a deep, living amber, the pigment drinking the sun with the enthusiasm of cells that had been repaired and were eager to prove it.

She did not look back. She knew that if she did, she would see Dzuvag standing at the cellar entrance with his staff, watching her the way the desert watched everything — without judgment, without sentiment, with the patient attention of a thing that would still be here when she was gone.

The orabaun crossed the morning sky, pearl by pearl. She set her course by them. West-northwest. Toward the Maya barrier. Toward the city that was going to burn, and the sister who would fight, and the sister who would stand in the fire, and the life she had left months ago as a child and was returning to as something else.

The desert did not oppose her. The desert let her pass.


---


# Chapter 20: The Lyonessa Returns

Anais had been waiting for forty-three days.

She came to the waterfront each morning when the twin suns rose and sat on the stone bench where the promenade met the harbor wall. The harbor was not a harbor anymore — the ships were gone, the docks had silted over, the cranes stood rusted in postures of arrested labor — but the water remained. A grey-green expanse, slow-moving, fed by underground springs that surfaced here at the continent's edge and pooled before draining into the deeper channels that led, eventually, to the sea. Herons stalked the shallows. Their legs were thin as reeds and their patience was immaculate, and Anais watched them the way she watched everything: with the quiet intensity of someone taking notes in a language she hadn't learned to write.

She wore a rain hood — transparent, the material a polymer that no one in Maya knew how to manufacture anymore. Auntie had given it to her. It was too large. It fell past her shoulders and pooled at her elbows and gave her the appearance, from a distance, of a small ghost wrapped in cellophane.

Auntie stood beside her. The automaton had not missed a single morning at the waterfront, her bronze-green face angled toward the western bend where the coastal path curved around the bluff and disappeared. She wore her pomegranate kaftan and her Hazzan-red headscarf and she stood with the absolute stillness that only a machine could sustain — no weight-shifting, no fidgeting, no adjustments. She watched the bend.

"She might not come today," Anais said. She said this every morning.

"She might," Auntie said. She said this every morning too. The ritual was not optimism. It was maintenance — the daily calibration of hope against evidence, performed with the same regularity as Auntie's joint lubrication and the herons' sunrise patrol.

The morning fog lifted. The water caught the light of Aleph — white, blinding — and the herons' shadows stretched long across the shallows. A wind came from the west, carrying salt and the mineral tang of the deeper channels and something else, something Anais could not name but that prickled along her arms like static.

A figure turned the bend.

---

It did not look like Aharah.

It looked like a thing the desert had tried to digest and failed. Dirty rags — the training wrap, reduced to strips, rewound over the body in improvised layers held together by knots and desperation. A limp: the right leg dragging slightly, the ankle swollen, the sandal on that foot held together by a strip of manok chitin. Ribs showing through the gaps in the wrapping. Sunskin dimmed to a matte gold that did not catch the light so much as absorb it. Hair brittle and grey at the ends, cropped short where the damaged strands had broken off. Across the figure's back, a sword in a makeshift sling. At the left hip, something Anais did not recognize: a chain, dark metal, coiled, with a weighted ball and a curved blade.

The figure moved slowly. Not the slowness of caution but the slowness of a body operating at the margin of its capacity, each step a negotiation between the will to continue and the physics of depleted muscle. The coastal path was stone — smooth, level, maintained by automatons that swept the surface nightly — and even this the figure navigated as if the ground might betray her.

Anais stood. The rain hood slipped from her shoulders.

"Auntie," she said.

But Auntie was already gone. The automaton moved — not walked, not ran, but launched, the bronze body covering the distance between the bench and the bend in a burst of speed that was terrifying in its silence and its precision. Auntie crossed fifty meters of promenade in seconds, her kaftan streaming behind her, her green-bronze face locked in an expression that was not programmed, that no engineer had designed, that had emerged from decades of something that was indistinguishable from love.

She reached the figure. She stopped. Her joints clicked — a rapid staccato, the sound of a machine trying to process information faster than its architecture allowed.

"Child," Auntie said.

The figure raised its head. The face beneath the filthy wrapping was gaunt. The cheekbones stood like ridges. The eyes — dark brown, ringed with amber — were sunk in hollows that the desert had carved. The lips were cracked. The jaw was set.

"Hello, Auntie," Aharah said.

Auntie's bronze hands — green-oxidized, the finger joints articulated with a precision that had not degraded in centuries — reached out and took Aharah's face. She held it. The metal palms against the sunskin cheeks, the machine's eternal coolness against the girl's desert-baked warmth. Auntie's chest rose and fell in the imitation of breath that no one had programmed.

"You are here," Auntie said.

"I am here."

Anais arrived. She did not sprint. She walked, because she was eight and because the gravity of the moment required a pace that matched its weight. She walked to her sister and she stood before her and she looked up at the face that the desert had changed — harder, leaner, older by years though only months had passed — and she said nothing. She took Aharah's hand. The hand was callused, the knuckles scarred, the fingers rough with new skin where the burns had healed. The gem-key pulsed at the wrist, steady now, its rhythm aligned with the heartbeat.

They walked together toward the barrier.

---

The Maya shimmered.

From outside, the barrier was heat-haze: a rippling distortion that turned the air liquid and the landscape behind it into a smear of color. Step through it without the blood-key in your marrow and the shimmer would redirect you — gently, imperceptibly, your thoughts rewriting themselves until you were certain there was nothing here, just ruins, just sand reclaiming stone. Step through it with the key and the shimmer parted like a curtain, and the city appeared.

Aharah stepped through. The barrier sang against her blood — a resonance in the marrow, the encoded frequency recognizing its own — and the ruins dissolved and Maya materialized around her. The sandstone spires. The cypress colonnades. The fountains singing their eternal phrase. The morning light hitting the ministerial quarter's pale walls and bouncing upward, filling the streets with a diffuse gold that made every surface glow.

She had forgotten how loud a city was. After months of desert silence — the dune-moan, the manok-click, the vast quiet between — Maya's morning noise struck her like a wall. Merchants calling. Cart wheels on stone. The clatter of an automaton sweeper working the fountain plaza. Children shrieking in a courtyard. A hammer ringing in the smithing quarter. All of it layered, simultaneous, the sound of thousands of lives conducted in proximity.

They moved through the streets. Auntie in front, clearing a path by presence alone — the automaton's military-grade frame commanding a deference that had nothing to do with authority and everything to do with the ancient bronze face and the click of joints that had been walking these streets since before anyone living was born. Aharah behind her, hood up, the training wraps covering the sunskin, the sword and chain-sickle hidden under the rags. Anais at her side, the rain hood back in place, her hand still holding Aharah's.

The rumors preceded them. How they traveled, Aharah could not see — she saw no signals, no runners, no messages passed between stalls. But by the time they reached the inner market, the city knew. Voices dropped as they passed. Heads turned. Eyes tracked the limping figure beneath the filthy hood.

"The Lyonessa is back."

She heard it first as a whisper, then as a murmur, then as a phrase repeated across the market stalls with the gathering force of a wave building toward shore.

"The Lyonessa returns."

"Without the Tagata—"

"She failed—"

"The vow—"

"She's doomed us all—"

---

The horse was dying.

It was not a horse — not the horses of the old myths, the Earth creatures that the seed-ship archives described. It was a buhaj: six-legged, barrel-chested, its hide a mottled grey, its head heavy and blunt with wide-set eyes that rolled in their sockets with the particular resignation of an animal that has accepted suffering as a weather condition. It lay on its side in the market square, its legs folded beneath it, its breath coming in shallow, wet rattles. Its ribs showed through the hide. Its hooves were split.

The man standing over it was hitting it with a crop.

The crop was a braided leather switch, knotted at the tip, and the man swung it with the mechanical regularity of someone performing a task rather than an act of cruelty — though the distinction was academic to the buhaj, whose hide flinched with each blow. The man was shouting. Something about a delivery. Something about the cost of fodder and the beast's refusal to stand.

The market crowd moved around the scene the way a stream moves around a stone — acknowledging its presence, avoiding its contact. No one intervened. The buhaj was property. Property could be struck.

Aharah stopped.

Anais felt the change before she saw it — a tension in the hand she held, the fingers tightening, the tendons in the wrist going taut. She looked up at her sister's face and saw the thing that happened before Aharah acted: the stillness. The absolute cessation of everything except intent.

"Aharah," Anais said. A warning.

Aharah released her hand. She walked into the square.

She did not run. She walked — the limping, deliberate walk of a body that was exhausted but that had made a decision the body could not overrule. She crossed the square in the bright morning light, and the crowd parted for her without knowing why, their bodies responding to something their minds had not yet registered.

She stepped between the man and the buhaj. The crop came down. She caught it. Her hand closed around the braided leather and held it in the air between them.

"Stop," she said.

The man stared at her. He saw rags. He saw filth. He saw a limping beggar half his size who had put herself between him and his dying animal.

"Get out of my way," he said.

"No."

He yanked the crop. She held it. The Lyonessa gene had given her a grip that could crack stone, and the man's yank moved her not at all. His face changed — confusion first, then outrage, then the specific anger of a man whose authority has been challenged by someone he considers beneath it.

He shoved her. Both hands, flat against her chest. The hood fell.

The sunskin caught the market light and blazed.

The gold was not the deep, living amber of health — not yet, not fully — but it was gold, and in the bright morning sun it flared along her face and neck and the exposed skin of her forearms with a radiance that was unmistakable. The Lyonessa pigment. The gene's signature. The mark of the Temple's chosen warrior, written in the skin itself.

The crowd went silent.

"The Lyonessa," someone said.

It spread. The word moved through the market the way fire moved through the dream — fast, channeled, following the contours of the crowd. "The Lyonessa." "She's back." "Look at her — she's starving." "She failed." "She broke the vow." "She's come to save us." "She's come to doom us."

The man with the crop stumbled backward. He dropped the switch. His face went white.

Aharah did not look at the crowd. She crouched beside the buhaj. She placed her hand on the animal's neck. The hide was hot and dry and the pulse beneath it was threadbare. The animal's eye rolled toward her — the wide, wet eye of a beast that could not name its suffering but could recognize the cessation of a blow.

The crowd broke.

---

It was not a riot in the way that organized violence is a riot. It was a rupture. The crowd's two halves — the half that worshipped the Lyonessa and the half that condemned her — found each other in the market square and the finding was not a meeting but a collision. A thrown stone. A shout. A merchant's stall overturned, the bolts of dyed cloth unrolling in the dust like bright tongues.

Aharah stood.

The crowd pressed in. Bodies and noise and the sharp copper smell of adrenaline. Someone grabbed her arm — not to help, to pull. She shook free. Someone else threw a stone and it struck her shoulder and the sunskin absorbed the impact the way it absorbed the sun — converting force to heat, the gold flaring at the point of contact.

She fought.
